Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate
7 min
Three Qur'anic Methods of Ifham and Ilzam in Debate
Author lists three methods used in Qur'an to silence and force opponents in debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three methods of ifham and ilzam used in Qur'anic debate: tahaddi (challenge), ilzam bi-muqtaḍā kalām (taking the opponent by his own words), and mujārāt (going along then rebutting).

Core Idea
The Qur'an silences opponents by using their own premises against them.

The Qur'an is not only a book of guidance but also a masterclass in debate. In several verses, it employs powerful dialectical methods to silence opponents and force them to confront the implications of their own statements. In this lesson, we explore three such methods: challenge (tahaddi), taking the opponent by his own words (ilzam bi-muqtaḍā kalām), and going along then rebutting (mujārāt). These strategies are not mere rhetoric but logical tools grounded in the opponent's own premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three methods
Read the passage and list the three methods: tahaddi, ilzam bi-muqtaḍā kalām al-khaṣm, and mujārāt.
2
Analyze the first method: tahaddi
Examine the example of Ibrahim (AS) challenging the king. How does the challenge work? It shifts the argument to an impossible task, demonstrating the opponent's inability.
The Qur'an also uses tahaddi against the disbelievers to produce ten surahs.
3
Analyze the second method: ilzam by own words
Look at the hypocrites' statement in 63:8. The verse concedes their premise but redefines who is truly 'honored', thus turning their claim against them.
4
Analyze the third method: mujārāt
Study the dialogue in 14:10-11. The messengers concede they are human but then add conditions (divine favor, permission) that rebut the people's objection.
This method involves partial agreement followed by a twist.
5
Compare the methods
Note that all three rely on accepting the opponent's premise and deriving a consequence that contradicts or refutes their position.
These are logical strategies, not emotional appeals.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا وكما استخدم القرآن أنواع الأدلة المختلفة لإقناع المخالفين، استخدم كذلك أساليب الإفحام والإلزام في المواضع التي تتطلبها، ويلاحظ أن القرآن الكريم في الجدل الذي يلزم الخصوم ويفحمهم يجيء إلى الإفحام من أقرب الطرق وأقواها إلزاما ومن ذلك ما حكاه الله تعالى عن خليله إبراهيم عليه السلام في مجادلة مدعي الألوهية، فقال تعالى: ﴿أَلَمْ تَرَ إِلَى الَّذِي حَاجَ إِبْرَاهِيمَ فِي رَبِّهِ أَنْ ءَاتَهُ اللهُ الْمُلْكَ إِذْ قَالَ إِبْرَاهِيمُ رَبِّيَ الَّذِي يُحْيِ، وَيُمِيتُ قَالَ أَنَا أَحْي وَأُمِيتُ قَالَ إِبْرَاهِيمُ فَإِنَّ اللهَ يَأْتِي بِالشَّمْسِ مِنَ الْمَشْرِقِ فَأْتِ بِهَا مِنَ الْمَغْرِبِ فَبُهِتَ الَّذِي كَفَرُّ وَاللهُ لَا يَهْدِى الْقَوْمَ الظَّلِمِينَ ) (1) [البقرة: 258]. وإن وسائل أخذ الخصم بأقرب طريق للإفحام والإلزام كثيرة : أ - منها التحدي، كما تحدى الله تعالى كفار قريش بأن يأتوا بعشر سور من مثل القرآن ،وكما تحدى إبراهيم الملك المدعي للألوهية. ب - ومنها أخذ الخصم بموجب كلامه وإثبات أنه عليه وليس له، قوله تعالى في شأن المنافقين : ﴿ يَقُولُونَ لَبِن رَجَعْنَا إِلَى الْمَدِينَةِ لَيُخْرِجَنَّ الْأَعَزُّ مِنْهَا الْأَذَلَّ وَلِلَّهِ الْعِزَّةُ وَلِرَسُولِهِ وَلِلْمُؤْمِنِينَ وَلَكِنَّ الْمُتَفِقِينَ لَا يَعْلَمُونَ﴾ (2) [المنافقون: 8]، فسلم لهم الله أن الأعز يخرج الأذل، ولكن من هو الأعز؟ ﴿ وَلِلَّهِ الْعِزَّةُ وَلِرَسُولِهِ وَالْمُؤْمِنِينَ . ج - ومنها مجاراة الخصم فيما يقول، ثم التعقيب عليه بما يقلب عليه نتائج ذلك قوله تعالى حاكيًا عن الرسل مع أقوامهم : قَالَتْ رُسُلُهُمْ أَفِي اللَّهِ شَءٌ فَاطِرِ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ يَدْعُوكُمْ لِيَغْفِرَ لَكُم مِّن ذُنُوبِكُمْ وَيُؤَخِّرَكُمْ إِلَى أَجَلٍ مُّسَمًّى قَالُواْ إِنْ أَنتُمْ إِلَّا بَشَرٌ مِثْلُنَا تُرِيدُونَ أَن تَصُدُّونَا عَمَّا كَانَ يَعْبُدُ ءَابَاؤُنَا فَأْتُونَا بِسُلْطَانٍ مُّبِين قَالَتْ لَهُمْ رُسُلُهُمْ إِن نَحْنُ إِلَّا بَشَرٌ مِثْلُكُمْ وَلَكِنَّ اللَّهَ يَمُنُّ عَلَى مَن يَشَاءُ مِنْ عِبَادِهِ، وَمَا كَانَ لَنَا أَن نَّأْتِيَكُم بِسُلْطَانٍ إِلَّا بِإِذْنِ اللَّهِ وَعَلَى اللَّهِ فَلْيَتَوَكَلِ الْمُؤْمِنُونَ [إبراهيم: 10-11].
And just as the Qur'an used various types of evidence to convince opponents, it also used methods of silencing (ifham) and forcing (ilzam) in contexts that required them. It is observed that the Noble Qur'an, in the dialectic that forces and silences opponents, comes to silencing by the closest and strongest means. Among that is what Allah, the Most High, related about His friend Ibrahim, peace be upon him, in his argument with the claimant of divinity. He said, the Most High: 'Have you not considered the one who argued with Ibrahim about his Lord because Allah had given him kingship? When Ibrahim said, My Lord is the one who gives life and causes death, he said, I give life and cause death. Ibrahim said, Indeed, Allah brings the sun from the east, so bring it from the west. So the one who disbelieved was dumbfounded. And Allah does not guide the wrongdoing people.' (2:258) And the means of taking the opponent by the closest path to silencing and forcing are many: (a) Among them is challenge (tahaddi), as Allah challenged the disbelievers of Quraysh to produce ten surahs like the Qur'an, and as Ibrahim challenged the king claiming divinity. (b) Among them is taking the opponent by the implication of his own statement and proving that it is against him and not for him, as His statement about the hypocrites: 'They say, If we return to Madinah, the more honored will surely expel the more humble. But to Allah belongs honor, and to His Messenger, and to the believers, but the hypocrites do not know.' (63:8) So Allah conceded to them that the more honored expels the more humble, but who is the more honored? 'To Allah belongs honor, and to His Messenger, and to the believers.' (c) Among them is going along with the opponent in what he says, then following up with something that turns the results against him, as His statement, the Most High, narrating from the messengers with their peoples: 'Their messengers said, Is there any doubt about Allah, the Originator of the heavens and the earth? He calls you to forgive you your sins and delay you for a specified term. They said, You are not but humans like us; you wish to avert us from what our fathers used to worship. So bring us a clear authority. Their messengers said to them, We are not but humans like you, but Allah bestows favor upon whomever He wills of His servants. And it is not for us to bring you an authority except by permission of Allah. And upon Allah let the believers rely.' (14:10-11).
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage discusses the methods of argumentation used in the Qur'an to silence (ifham) and force (ilzam) opponents. The author observes that the Qur'an employs the closest and most effective means to achieve this. The first example given is the famous debate between Prophet Ibrahim and Nimrod (or a tyrant king) recorded in Surah al-Baqarah (2:258). When the king claimed he could give life and death, Ibrahim shifted the argument by challenging him to make the sun rise from the west, thereby exposing the king's impotence. This demonstrates the method of challenge (tahaddi). The author then enumerates three specific means: (a) Challenge (tahaddi): The Qur'an challenges the disbelievers to produce ten surahs like it, and Ibrahim challenged the king. (b) Taking the opponent by the implication of his own words: The author cites the hypocrites' statement in Surah al-Munafiqun (63:8) that 'the more honored will expel the more humble.' The Qur'an accepts this premise but then redefines who is truly honored—Allah, His Messenger, and the believers—showing that the hypocrites' claim actually works against them. (c) Going along with the opponent then rebutting: The author references the dialogue between messengers and their peoples in Surah Ibrahim (14:10-11). The people argue that the messengers are merely human like them. The messengers concede that they are human but then emphasize that Allah bestows favor on whom He wills and that miracles come only by Allah's permission, thus turning the argument back on the people. Each method is a practical application of dialectical strategy rooted in Qur'anic examples.

الإفحام
الإلزام
التحدي

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an's dialectical methods—challenge, turning the opponent's words against him, and conceding then rebutting—are timeless logical tools for effective debate.

Dialectic -- Jadal

The Prophetic Jadal Strategy: Accepting Premises, Rejecting Conclusions
Author illustrates the Quranic jadal method where prophets accept the premise but reject the false conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the dialectical strategy of accepting a true premise while rejecting a false conclusion in Quranic debates.

Core Idea
Prophets win jadal by granting the premise but invalidating the false inference.

In this passage, the author reveals a powerful dialectical technique used by the prophets in the Quran: they concede a true premise raised by their opponents, but then demonstrate why the conclusion drawn from it does not follow. This method allows them to dismantle false arguments without conceding the overall claim. By studying this, you will learn how to apply a similar logical move in your own debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the accepted premise
Find the statement that the prophets agree with. In the example, the opponents say 'you are humans', and the prophets accept this as true.
2
Identify the false conclusion
What do the opponents intend to conclude from that premise? They want to prove the prophets are not chosen by God. The prophets reject this conclusion.
3
See how the prophets refute the inference
The prophets invoke a higher principle: God chooses whom He wills. This shows the premise does not necessarily lead to the conclusion; there is a missing element (God's will).
4
Generalize the strategy
Understand that this move works by demonstrating an invalid inference. The debater does not need to deny the truth of the opponent's premise; rather, they show the conclusion does not follow.
This is akin to denying the consequent or highlighting a missing premise in formal logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنرى من الجدل هذا النص السامي أن الرسل سلموا بالمقدمة التي بنى عليها الأقوام رفضهم، ولكنهم نقضوا النتيجة بقولهم ... وَلَكِنَّ اللَّهَ يَمُنُّ عَلَى مَن يَشَاءُ مِنْ عِبَادِهِ ...، فكأنهم قالوا لهم ما قلتموه من أننا بشر حق، ولكن ما تريدون أن تبنوا عليه من إثبات أننا لسنا أنبياء باطل، لأن الله يمن على من يشاء من [؟] قد عباده، وقدمنا لكم السلطان أي الدليل، ولا سلطان لنا إلا ما قد عباده، وهو يأذن به الله تعالى. وهكذا، فإن الدارس المتمعن يقف في القرآن الكريم على منهج متكامل في الجدل، يتضمن الطرق التي ينبغي السير عليها والأدلة التي يتوجب استخدامها، والآداب التي من الضروري الالتزام بها والمسالك السيئة التي لابد له من الحياد عنها، ولا شك أن الالتزام بهذا المنهج كفيل بتحقيق أحسن النتائج في كل مجال يخوض فيه الإنسان جدلا مع غيره.
We see from jadal this sublime text that the messengers agreed to the premise upon which the peoples built their rejection, but they refuted the conclusion by saying: 'But Allah bestows His favor upon whom He wills of His servants'... as if they said to them: 'What you said that we are humans is true, but what you want to build upon it of proving that we are not prophets is false, because Allah bestows favor upon whom He wills of His servants... and we have presented to you the authority, i.e., the proof, and we have no authority except what has [?] His servants, and it is permitted by Allah Almighty.' Thus, the careful student finds in the Noble Quran a complete methodology of jadal, including the methods that should be followed, the proofs that must be used, the etiquettes that must be adhered to, and the bad paths that must be avoided. There is no doubt that adhering to this methodology guarantees achieving the best results in every field in which a person engages in jadal with others.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage demonstrates a key dialectical strategy used by the prophets in the Quran: accepting a true premise from the opponents but denying the false conclusion they draw from it. The author provides an example where disbelievers argue that prophets are mere humans (a true premise), intending to conclude that they cannot be prophets. The prophets concede the premise but reject the conclusion by invoking God's prerogative to choose whomever He wills. This move shows that jadal can be a legitimate method for defending truth when conducted properly. The author then generalizes that the Quran contains a complete methodology for jadal, covering methods, proofs, etiquettes, and pitfalls. This should be studied carefully.

Key Terms
جدل (jadal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are not prophets.
B) They are humans.
C) They have no authority.
D) They are angels.

Key Takeaway
The prophets' jadal method shows that conceding a true premise can be an effective way to expose a false inference and win the argument.

The Prophetic Condemnation of Disputation in the Qur'an
Author cites hadith condemning jadal in the Qur'an.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between blameworthy jadal (disputation in matters of divine revelation) and permissible debate based on hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Disputing the Qur'an is disbelief; disputation can lead communities astray.

This lesson examines the strong condemnation of jadal (disputation) found in the Prophetic hadith. The author shows that the root j-d-l appears in hadith with negative connotations such as denial and censure, especially when applied to the Qur'an. Understanding which forms of jadal are forbidden is crucial to distinguishing between legitimate theological debate and forbidden contention.

Guided Path
1
Locate the root j-d-l in the hadith texts
In the Arabic, find the words جدال (jidal), مراء (mira'), and أوتوا الجدل (utoo al-jadal). These are the key terms indicating condemnation.
The root appears at least nineteen times in hadith, mostly in negative contexts.
2
Identify the object of the disputation
The hadith specify 'في القرآن' (in the Qur'an) — the condemnation is directed at disputing the divine revelation itself.
3
Note the consequences mentioned
The hadith declare disputation in the Qur'an as 'كفر' (disbelief) and associate it with going astray from guidance.
This shows the severity: it is not a minor sin but a rejection of faith.
4
Distinguish from other forms of debate
Understand that this condemnation is specific to contentious argument about the Qur'an. Other forms of jadal (e.g., in usul al-fiqh) may be permissible or even obligatory.
Later scholars differentiated between jadal madhmum and jadal mamduh.

The third section: Jadal in the noble Prophetic Sunnah. First requirement: The lexical material of jadal in the verbal Sunnah. The word 'j-d-l' occurs in the hadith at least nineteen times, as derived from the concordance of prophetic hadith by Wensinck, except that it comes with meanings indicating denial, prevention, and censure upon those who take disputation as a means to confront others who disagree with them. And in another narration from Abu Huraira also: 'Arguing in the Qur'an is disbelief.' And in another wording: 'Arguing in the Qur'an is disbelief.' And from Abu Umama, may Allah be pleased with him, he said: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'No people went astray after guidance they were upon except that they were given disputation,' then the Messenger of Allah recited this verse: 'They strike it for you...'

This passage surveys the occurrence of the root j-d-l in hadith literature. The author notes that it appears nineteen times, predominantly with negative meanings such as denial (inkar), prevention (man'), and censure (tashni'). Three hadith are cited: two from Abu Huraira stating that disputing (jidal) in the Qur'an is kufr, and one from Abu Umama where the Prophet links going astray to being given disputation, then recites a Quranic verse. This demonstrates that jadal is heavily condemned when it involves contention over divine revelation.

Blameworthy vs. Praiseworthy Jadal in Hadith
Author presents hadith evidence against jadal in religion and distinguishes blameworthy from praiseworthy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between blameworthy jadal (al-jadal al-madhmūm) and praiseworthy jadal (al-jadal al-maḥmūd) based on the Prophetic hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Not all jadal is forbidden; only blameworthy jadal that causes division is prohibited.

The Prophet ﷺ warned against jadal that leads to discord, but he himself engaged in praiseworthy debate. This lesson unpacks the hadith evidence and the crucial distinction between blameworthy and praiseworthy jadal.

Guided Path
1
Locate the hadith evidence
Find the three key hadiths cited in the passage: the hadith that 'jadal in the Quran is kufr', the hadith about 'mirā' in the Quran', and the hadith 'No people went astray after guidance except they were given jadal'.
2
Identify the negative connotation
Note that in each hadith, the term jadal (or mirā') is associated with disbelief, misguidance, or contentiousness. The author states these meanings imply denial, prevention, and disparagement.
3
Understand the rationale for prohibition
The author explains that the Prophet prohibited jadal in religion to preserve Muslim unity and avoid the sectarianism that afflicted Jews and Christians. This prohibition targets jadal that leads to conflict, especially in creedal matters.
4
Recognize the exception for praiseworthy jadal
The author explicitly states that the Prophet only prohibits blameworthy jadal. Praiseworthy jadal, which the Quran urges for da'wa to non-Muslims and which benefits Islam, is not only permitted but practiced by the Prophet himself.
This distinction is foundational in Islamic dialectics (ʿilm al-jadal).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث : الجدل في السنة النبوية الشريفة المطلب الأول : مادة الجدل في السنة القولية وردت كلمة «جدل» في الحديث تسعة عشر مرة على الأقل، وذلك المستفاد من المعجم المفهرس لألفاظ الحديث النبوي لفنسنك، إلا أنها جاءت بمعاني تفيد الإنكار والمنع والتشنيع على من يتخذ من الجدل وسيلة في مواجهة الغير ممن لا يتفقون معه في الرأي ... من ذلك، ما رواه الإمام أحمد في مسنده، عن أبي هريرة عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ، أنه قال : «جدال في القرآن كفر» وفي رواية أخرى عن أبي هريرة أيضًا، قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «المراء في القرآن كفر، وفي لفظ آخر : مراء في القرآن كفر وعن أبي أمامة رضي الله عنه قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «ما ضل قوم بعد هدى كانوا عليه إلا أوتوا الجدل ) ، ثم تلا رسول الله له هذه الآية : مَا ضَرَبُوهُ لَكَ إِلَّا جَدَلًا بَلْ هُوَ قَوْمُ خَصِمُونَ)... فهذه النصوص كلها صريحة إذا كان هذا النزاع في ديني خطير . ورسول الله عليه الصلاة والسلام يعرف جيدا كيف أن الجدل في المسائل الدينية، وخاصة منها ما كان متعلقا بالغيبيات كان سببا أساسا في تفرق أهل الكتاب من اليهود والنصارى من قبل وتشتتهم إلى شيع ومذاهب يضرب بعضها بعضا، وهو ما أراد أن يجنب المسلمين ويلاته ويقيهم شروره. فالنهي النبوي عن الجدل بين المسلمين يخفي في طياته الحرص على صرفهم عن التجادل فيما بينهم، إذ لا يحتمل أن ينجر عنه إلا ما يفرق صفوفهم، خاصة إذا كان له مساس بقضايا العقيدة المعضلة والمتعلقة بالروح أو الآخرة، أو حتى بمجرد مسائل تأويل بعض الآيات القرآنية التي اختلف المسلمون في فهمها» لكن هذا لا يعني أن النبي ينهي عن الجدل مهما كان نوعه، ومهما كان فيه فائدة للإسلام والمسلمين فالنبي عليه الصلاة والسلام، إنما ينهى عن الجدل المذموم الذي لا نتيجة له سوى بث أسباب الفرقة والخصام والشقاق بين المسلمين، أما الجدل المحمود الذي حث عليه القرآن المسلمين في التعامل مع من هم على غير دينهم والذي لا يمكن أن يتمخض إلا عن كل ما ينفع الإسلام وأهله ، فإن النبي لا ينهى عنه ، بل إنه يمارسه هو شخصيا في سنته العملية في التعامل مع خصوم الإسلام من المشركين وأهل الكتاب.
Third Section: Dialectics in the Noble Prophetic Sunnah. First Requirement: The Material of Dialectics in the Proverbial Sunnah. The word 'jadal' occurs in hadith at least nineteen times, as per the Concordance of Prophetic Hadith by Wensinck, but it came with meanings implying denial, prevention, and disparagement against those who take dialectics as a means in confronting others who do not agree with them in opinion... From that is what Imam Ahmad narrated in his Musnad, from Abu Huraira from the Prophet, that he said: 'Dialectics in the Quran is disbelief.' In another narration from Abu Huraira as well, the Messenger of God said: 'Argumentation in the Quran is disbelief.' In another wording: 'Argumentation in the Quran is disbelief.' And from Abu Umama, may God be pleased with him, said: The Messenger of God said: 'No people went astray after guidance they were upon except they were given dialectics,' then the Messenger of God recited this verse: 'They strike it not as an example except in dialectics; rather, they are a contentious people.' Thus all these texts are explicit in prohibiting dialectics in religion and contention and dispute in its matters. The explanation of that is that the Prophet was keen on the unity of the Muslims, which is preserved by repelling everything that would lead to conflict and contention, especially if this conflict is in a dangerous religious matter. The Messenger of God knows well how dialectics in religious matters, especially those related to the unseen, was a primary cause of the dispersion of the People of the Book from Jews and Christians before and their fragmentation into sects and schools striking each other, which he wanted to spare the Muslims its calamities and protect them from its evils. So the prophetic prohibition of dialectics among Muslims conceals within it the care to divert them from debating among themselves, because it cannot result in anything except dividing their ranks, especially if it touches difficult issues of creed related to the soul or the afterlife, or even mere issues of interpreting some Quranic verses that Muslims differed in understanding. But this does not mean that the Prophet prohibits dialectics no matter what its type, and whatever benefit it may have for Islam and Muslims. Rather, the Prophet only prohibits blameworthy dialectics that has no result except spreading causes of division, contention, and discord among Muslims. As for praiseworthy dialectics that the Quran urged Muslims to use in dealing with those who are not of their religion, and which can only result in what benefits Islam and its people, the Prophet does not prohibit it; rather he personally practices it in his practical Sunnah in dealing with opponents of Islam from the polytheists and People of the Book.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exposition of the Prophetic evidence regarding jadal. It opens by stating that the word 'jadal' appears in hadith at least nineteen times, and that these occurrences carry negative connotations of denial and reproach against those who use jadal to confront others. The author then cites three key hadiths: first, 'Jidal in the Quran is kufr' (from Abu Huraira via Imam Ahmad); second, 'Al-mira' in the Quran is kufr' (another wording); third, 'No people went astray after guidance except they were given jadal,' followed by the Prophet reciting verse 43:58 about the polytheists being a contentious people. The author explains that these hadiths clearly forbid jadal in religious matters because the Prophet was deeply concerned about Muslim unity. He knew that jadal in religion, especially regarding the unseen, had caused the earlier Jews and Christians to split into sects. Therefore, the prohibition aims to prevent division. However, the author then makes a crucial distinction: the Prophet only prohibits blameworthy jadal (al-jadal al-madhmum) that leads to disunity and discord. As for praiseworthy jadal (al-jadal al-mahmud), which the Quran encourages for da'wa to non-Muslims, the Prophet not only permits it but practiced it himself. This distinction between mazmum and mahmud jadal is fundamental in Islamic dialectics, showing that the discipline of jadal is not inherently condemned; rather, its legitimacy depends on intention, subject matter, and outcome.

مِيرَاء
جَدَل مَذْمُوم
جَدَل مَحْمُود

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's prohibition of jadal is conditional; blameworthy jadal causes division, while praiseworthy jadal is practiced by the Prophet himself.

Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate
5 min
Distinguishing Praiseworthy and Blameworthy Jadal
Author classifies jadal into blameworthy and praiseworthy based on hadith and scholarly commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between praiseworthy and blameworthy jadal based on intention and context, using the hadith and scholarly commentary.

Core Idea
Jadal is blameworthy when based on falsehood; praiseworthy when seeking truth with proper adab.

This lesson examines the key distinction between blameworthy and praiseworthy disputation (jadal) as clarified by the Prophet's hadith and subsequent commentaries. You will learn how intention and method determine whether jadal is prohibited or commended in Islam, and how to apply this distinction to real theological and legal debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith and its apparent prohibition
The Prophet said: 'No people have gone astray after being guided except that they were given jadal.' This seems to condemn jadal universally. Read the hadith carefully and note its general wording.
This is the starting point for the classification.
2
Extract the lexical definition of jadal
The author defines jadal as 'pitting proof against proof' (muqābalat al-ḥujja bi-l-ḥujja). This is a neutral definition – it does not yet distinguish good from bad. Recognize that the term itself is neutral.
The definition comes from linguistic usage, not a technical classification.
3
Recognize the two types based on intention
The author divides jadal into two categories: jadal 'alā al-bāṭil (upon falsehood) with the intention of domination, and jadal li-iẓhār al-ḥaqq (to manifest truth). The hadith's prohibition applies only to the first type. The Quran (16:125) commands the second type.
Intention is the key differentia.
4
Analyze the scholarly commentary
Al-Munawi explains that the hadith refers to those who abandon guidance and persist in false contention. Al-Qadi specifies that it targets partisanship for false creeds, not debate for truth, learning, or teaching. Ibn al-Athir adds that the prohibition may be limited to theological speculation (qadr), not fiqh. These commentaries refine the blanket prohibition.
The commentators restrict the scope of the hadith.
5
Apply the rule to classify jadal
Now you can classify any instance of jadal: Is it based on falsehood? Aimed at domination? If yes, it is blameworthy. Is it for truth, with proper adab and taqwa, only as needed? Then it is praiseworthy, even in subsidiary fiqh matters.
This step synthesizes the lesson's tool.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حديث النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام السابق الذكر : (ما ضل قوم بعد هدى كانوا عليه إلا أوتوا الجدل)، حيث قال : «الجدل : مقابلة الحجة بالحجة والمجادلة : المناظرة والمخاصمة، والمراد به في الحديث : الجدل على الباطل، وطلب المغالبة به، فأما الجدل لإظهار الحق فإن ذلك محمود لقوله تعالى : ﴿وَجَادِلْهُم بِالَّتِي هِيَ أَحْسَنُ﴾ . وهو ما يؤكده أيضًا الإمام المناوي، إذ يقول في التعليق على هذا الحديث : «أي ؛ ما ضل قوم مهديون كائنين على حال من الأحوال إلا أوتوا الجدل، يعني : من ترك سبيل الهدى وركب سنن الضلالة والمراد : لم يمش حاله إلا بالجدل أي الخصومة بالباطل، وقال القاضي : المراد التعصب لترويج المذاهب الكاسدة والعقائد الزائفة لا المناظرة لإظهار الحق واستكشاف الحال و استعلام ما ليس معلوما عنده أو تعليم غيره ما عنده لأنه فرض كفاية خارج عما نطق به الحديث». فالجدل إذن كمنهج لمحاجة خصوم العقيدة أو من يؤمل منهم اعتناقها هو جدل محمود، وليس هو المراد في النهي الوارد عن الجدل في السنة النبوية القولية. بل إن الجدل في فروع الشريعة الإسلامية ذاتها هو جدل محمود إذا استصحب الآداب الشرعية الشريفة وتقوى الله، ولم يكن المراد منه سوى الوصول إلى الحق في المسائل الفرعية المختلف فيها. كل هذا يدلنا على أن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام لقد نهى عن الجدل الذي لا يكون مقصودًا منه إلا الجدل في ذاته. أما الجدل الذي يكون عند الحاجة وعلى قدرها فهو جدل محمود بل مطلوب وهذا ما يؤكد عليه ابن الأثير أيضًا حين ينقل عن بعض العلماء تعليقهم على أحد الأحاديث الناهية عن الجدل في الدين : وقال بعضهم: إنما جاء هذا في الجدال والمراء في الآيات التي فيها ذكر القدر ونحوه من المعاني، على مذهب أهل الكلام، دون ما تضمنته من الأحكام وأبواب التحليل والتحريم، فإن ذلك قد جرى بين الصحابة رضي الله عنهم ومن بعدهم من العلماء، وليس ذلك محظورًا، والله تعالى أعلم.
The previously mentioned hadith of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'No people have gone astray after being guided except that they were given disputation (jadal).' Then he said: 'Jadal is pitting proof against proof, and mujadalah is debate and quarrel. What is intended in the hadith is disputation upon falsehood and seeking to dominate through it. As for disputation to manifest the truth, that is praiseworthy, due to His statement, the Exalted: 'And dispute with them in a way that is best.' This is also affirmed by Imam al-Munawi when he comments on this hadith: 'That is, no guided people have been in any state except that they were given jadal, meaning: whoever abandons the path of guidance and follows the ways of misguidance—and the point is that his state does not proceed except with jadal, i.e., contention with falsehood. Al-Qadi said: What is intended is partisanship to promote bankrupt schools and false creeds, not debate to manifest truth, investigate the matter, learn what is unknown to one, or teach others what one knows, because that is a communal obligation outside what the hadith speaks of.' Thus jadal as a method for disputing opponents of the creed or those hoped to embrace it is praiseworthy jadal, and it is not intended by the prohibition of jadal in the Prophetic oral sunna. Rather, jadal in the branches of Islamic law itself is praiseworthy jadal if it is accompanied by noble legal etiquettes and piety towards Allah, and its only intention is reaching truth in the subsidiary matters of disagreement. All this indicates that the Prophet, peace be upon him, prohibited jadal whose only purpose is jadal itself. As for jadal when needed and to the extent needed, it is praiseworthy, indeed demanded. This is affirmed by Ibn al-Athir as well when he transmits from some scholars their commentary on one of the hadiths prohibiting jadal in religion: 'Some of them said: This applies only to jidal and mira' concerning verses mentioning predestination and similar meanings according to the doctrine of the theologians (ahl al-kalam), not what is entailed by rulings and chapters of permissible and forbidden (halal and haram), for indeed that (debate about rulings) has occurred among the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, and among those after them from the scholars, and that is not prohibited. And Allah knows best.'
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage defines the term jadal (disputation) and establishes the distinction between blameworthy and praiseworthy forms of argumentation. It opens by citing a well-known hadith that links going astray to having been given the capacity for jadal. The author then clarifies the lexical meaning of jadal as 'pitting proof against proof' and mujadalah as 'debate and quarrel'. The key point is that the hadith's condemnation applies specifically to disputation based on falsehood and the desire for domination. However, disputation aimed at manifesting the truth is commended by the Quranic verse 'And dispute with them in a way that is best' (16:125). The author reinforces this with a commentary from al-Munawi, who explains that the hadith refers to those who abandon guidance and persist in false contention. Al-Munawi quotes a Qadi who distinguishes between partisanship for falsehood (which is blameworthy) and legitimate debate for uncovering truth, learning, or teaching (which is a communal obligation). The passage then concludes that jadal used to defend the Islamic creed is praiseworthy, as is jadal in subsidiary matters of fiqh when conducted with proper adab and sincerity. The Prophet's prohibition targets only jadal for its own sake, not jadal that serves a genuine need. This is further supported by Ibn al-Athir's transmission that certain scholars restrict the prohibition to jadal about predestination and theological speculation, not practical legal matters, for which the Companions themselves engaged in debate. Thus, the passage guards against the misunderstanding that all forms of jadal are prohibited.

Key Takeaway
Jadal is not inherently prohibited; it is blameworthy when based on falsehood and praiseworthy when intended to manifest truth with proper adab and piety.

Defining Praiseworthy Jadal and Its Conditions
Definition and criteria of praiseworthy jadal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish praiseworthy jadal from blameworthy jadal by identifying its intention, method, and subject matter.

Core Idea
Jadal is only blameworthy when done for its own sake; it is praiseworthy when seeking truth with proper etiquette.

The author begins by clarifying a common misconception: not all jadal is forbidden. He outlines the criteria that transform debate from a blameworthy activity into a praiseworthy one, grounded in intention and adherence to Islamic etiquettes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of jadal
Find in the passage the distinction between the prohibited and the praiseworthy. Look for keywords like 'جدل محمود' and 'نهي'. What does the author say about each?
2
Extract the conditions for praiseworthy jadal
What does the author list as necessary for jadal to be praiseworthy? Consider intention (الوصول إلى الحق), method (الآداب الشرعية), and piety (تقوى الله).
These conditions are given in the first paragraph.
3
Understand the scope of the prohibition
According to the quote from Ibn al-Athir, the prophetic prohibition is limited. What topics are excluded from the prohibition?
The exclusion includes debates about legal rulings (أبواب التحليل والتحريم).
4
Apply the distinction
Practice recognizing whether a given debate scenario would be praiseworthy or blameworthy based on these criteria.

سير أعلام النبلاء للذهبي،
Jadal: praiseworthy jadal, and it is not what is intended in the prohibition that came about jadal in the prophetic verbal Sunnah. Rather, jadal in the branches of Islamic law itself is praiseworthy jadal if it accompanies the noble legal etiquettes and piety towards Allah, and its only purpose is to reach the truth in subsidiary matters of disagreement. All of this indicates to us that the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited jadal that has no purpose except jadal for its own sake. As for jadal that arises when needed and to the extent required, it is praiseworthy jadal, nay, required. This is also affirmed by Ibn al-Athir when he reports from some scholars their commentary on one of the hadiths prohibiting jadal: 'Some said: This (prohibition) is only about contention and disputation in verses that contain mention of predestination and the like of meanings, according to the way of the theologians, not regarding what the verses contain of rulings and chapters of lawful and forbidden, for that (debate) occurred among the Companions and those after them from the scholars, and it is not prohibited. And Allah knows best.'
Second demand: Applications of jadal in the practical Sunnah
We have seen, therefore, that jadal intended for supporting the Islamic creed is praiseworthy jadal, and it does not fall under the prohibition in the verbal prophetic Sunnah. This is confirmed by the actual practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) in his call to non-Muslims. That call which naturally led to the emergence of jadal between him (peace be upon him) and those he called regarding this call and what it carried of doctrinal and legislative elements, and which the Prophet (peace be upon him) did not hesitate to engage in, as long as his call required it. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) only proceeded in that from the Noble Quran, submitting to its commands and directives that emphasize opening the door to dialogue and jadal with non-Muslims, explaining the rulings of creed and law to them and managing speech with them concerning it, perhaps they may be guided to the straight path.
This is what the Prophet (peace be upon him) practiced in his call to the polytheists, his call to the Jews, and his call to the Christians:
A - The Prophet's jadal with the polytheists: -
At the beginning of the call, the Prophet (peace be upon him) was keen to keep it secret, inclining to choose those to call; but the matter did not remain for him in this state, for the command came from his Lord about the necessity of declaring the call openly and announcing it before the witnesses. So he (peace be upon him) carried out what his Lord commanded him, calling to his Lord with goodness, and explaining to his people the creed of Islam in the best way. In this open call, Quraysh were provoked when the Prophet (peace be upon him) mentioned their gods badly and explained the invalidity of worshipping them, so they came debating, but the debate by tongue defeated them, though they were a contentious people. So they resorted to mockery and ridicule, and incited the foolish against him. Then the matter moved from debate and argument with proof to persecution and boycott of the Prophet (peace be upon him), as we know in the prophetic biography. It came in the Sira of Ibn Hisham that the polytheists, when they were distressed by the Prophet (peace be upon him) and every trick had failed with him, sent to him to speak to him and dispute with him. So he (peace be upon him) came to them, and they said: O Muhammad, we have sent to you to speak to you. By Allah, we do not know any man from the Arabs who has brought to his people what you have brought to your people. You have insulted the forefathers, criticized the religion, reviled the gods, called the intellect foolish, and divided the community. There is no ugly thing left but you have done it between us and you. If you have come with this speech seeking wealth, we will collect for you from our wealth until you become the wealthiest among us. If you are seeking honor among us, we will make you our chief. And if...
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a definition and illustration of praiseworthy jadal (dialectical debate) in the Islamic tradition. The author begins by distinguishing two types of jadal: one that is blameworthy and prohibited in the prophetic hadith, and another that is praiseworthy and even required when done with proper Islamic etiquettes and piety, solely to reach the truth in subsidiary legal matters. The author clarifies that the prophetic prohibition only targets jadal for its own sake, not jadal that is necessary for defending the truth. This is supported by a quote from Ibn al-Athir, who relates that scholars interpret the relevant hadith as restricting the prohibition to theological debates about predestination and similar topics, while excluding debates about legal rulings which the Companions engaged in without prohibition.

Then the author moves to the practical application of praiseworthy jadal in the Sunnah, specifically in the Prophet's invitation of non-Muslims. The Prophet's practice shows that jadal for supporting the Islamic creed is not only allowed but exemplified, as he engaged in dialogue and debate with polytheists, Jews, and Christians as commanded by the Quran. The author details the Prophet's debate with the polytheists of Quraysh, describing how after the open call, they came to debate but were intellectually defeated, then resorted to mockery and persecution. A narrative from Ibn Hisham's Sira is cited, where the polytheists confront the Prophet listing their grievances about his teachings affecting their religion, unity, and ancestral traditions, and they offer him wealth and leadership in an attempt to bring him back. The lesson underscores that jadal is legitimate and praiseworthy when used for the sake of Allah and to convey the truth.

The Prophet's Debate with Quraysh: A Case Study in Praiseworthy Jadal
Application of praiseworthy jadal in prophetic biography

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the structure of the Prophet's debate with Quraysh using the framework of jadal and identify the dialectical moves.

Core Idea
The Prophet's debate with Quraysh exemplifies praiseworthy jadal: defending truth with patience while opponents resort to mockery and offers.

After defining praiseworthy jadal, the author turns to the Prophet's actual practice, showing how he engaged in debate with polytheists. The narrative from Ibn Hisham illustrates the dialectical exchange and the correct prophetic response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the occasion
What prompted the debate? Why did Quraysh initiate the meeting? The passage mentions that they were distressed and every trick had failed.
2
List the accusations
What specific grievances did Quraysh raise against the Prophet? They mention insulting forefathers, criticizing the religion, reviling gods, etc.
3
Analyze the Prophet's response
Although the passage does not give the Prophet's exact words, what is his implied response based on his mission? He continued to call to the truth and did not accept their offers.
4
Evaluate the outcome
What was the polytheists' reaction when debate failed? They resorted to mockery, ridicule, and later persecution.

بالتي هي أحسن . ولقد كان في هذه الدعوة الجهرية ما استفز قريشًا حين ذكر النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام آلهتها بسوء وبين بطلان عبادتها فأقبلوا مجادلين ولكن الجدال باللسان ،أعجزهم وهم القوم الخصمون، فعمدوا إلى الاستهزاء والسخرية، وأغروا السفهاء به ، ثم انتقل الأمر من جدل ومقارعة بالحجة إلى اضطهاد ومقاطعة للنبي عليه الصلاة والسلام مما نعلم أمره في السيرة النبوية . جاء في سيرة ابن هشام (1) أن المشركين عندما ضاقوا بالنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وذهبت معه كل حيلة لهم، وبعثوا إليه ليكلموه ويخاصموه، فجاء إليهم عليه الصلاة والسلام فقالوا له : يا محمد إنا قد بعثنا إليك لنكلمك ، وإنا والله ما نعلم رجلا من العرب أدخل على قومه مثل ما أدخلت على قومك، لقد شتمت الآباء وعبت الدين و شتمت الآلهة وسفهت الأحلام وفرقت الجماعة، فما بقي أمر قبيح إلا جئته فيما بيننا وبينك فإن كنت إنما جئت بهذا الحديث تطلب مالا جمعنا لك من أموالنا حتى تكون أكثرنا مالا ، وإن كنت إنما تطلب الشرف فينا فنحن نسودك علينا، وإن

سير أعلام النبلاء للذهبي،
Jadal: praiseworthy jadal, and it is not what is intended in the prohibition that came about jadal in the prophetic verbal Sunnah. Rather, jadal in the branches of Islamic law itself is praiseworthy jadal if it accompanies the noble legal etiquettes and piety towards Allah, and its only purpose is to reach the truth in subsidiary matters of disagreement. All of this indicates to us that the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited jadal that has no purpose except jadal for its own sake. As for jadal that arises when needed and to the extent required, it is praiseworthy jadal, nay, required. This is also affirmed by Ibn al-Athir when he reports from some scholars their commentary on one of the hadiths prohibiting jadal: 'Some said: This (prohibition) is only about contention and disputation in verses that contain mention of predestination and the like of meanings, according to the way of the theologians, not regarding what the verses contain of rulings and chapters of lawful and forbidden, for that (debate) occurred among the Companions and those after them from the scholars, and it is not prohibited. And Allah knows best.'
Second demand: Applications of jadal in the practical Sunnah
We have seen, therefore, that jadal intended for supporting the Islamic creed is praiseworthy jadal, and it does not fall under the prohibition in the verbal prophetic Sunnah. This is confirmed by the actual practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) in his call to non-Muslims. That call which naturally led to the emergence of jadal between him (peace be upon him) and those he called regarding this call and what it carried of doctrinal and legislative elements, and which the Prophet (peace be upon him) did not hesitate to engage in, as long as his call required it. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) only proceeded in that from the Noble Quran, submitting to its commands and directives that emphasize opening the door to dialogue and jadal with non-Muslims, explaining the rulings of creed and law to them and managing speech with them concerning it, perhaps they may be guided to the straight path.
This is what the Prophet (peace be upon him) practiced in his call to the polytheists, his call to the Jews, and his call to the Christians:
A - The Prophet's jadal with the polytheists: -
At the beginning of the call, the Prophet (peace be upon him) was keen to keep it secret, inclining to choose those to call; but the matter did not remain for him in this state, for the command came from his Lord about the necessity of declaring the call openly and announcing it before the witnesses. So he (peace be upon him) carried out what his Lord commanded him, calling to his Lord with goodness, and explaining to his people the creed of Islam in the best way. In this open call, Quraysh were provoked when the Prophet (peace be upon him) mentioned their gods badly and explained the invalidity of worshipping them, so they came debating, but the debate by tongue defeated them, though they were a contentious people. So they resorted to mockery and ridicule, and incited the foolish against him. Then the matter moved from debate and argument with proof to persecution and boycott of the Prophet (peace be upon him), as we know in the prophetic biography. It came in the Sira of Ibn Hisham that the polytheists, when they were distressed by the Prophet (peace be upon him) and every trick had failed with him, sent to him to speak to him and dispute with him. So he (peace be upon him) came to them, and they said: O Muhammad, we have sent to you to speak to you. By Allah, we do not know any man from the Arabs who has brought to his people what you have brought to your people. You have insulted the forefathers, criticized the religion, reviled the gods, called the intellect foolish, and divided the community. There is no ugly thing left but you have done it between us and you. If you have come with this speech seeking wealth, we will collect for you from our wealth until you become the wealthiest among us. If you are seeking honor among us, we will make you our chief. And if...
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a definition and illustration of praiseworthy jadal (dialectical debate) in the Islamic tradition. The author begins by distinguishing two types of jadal: one that is blameworthy and prohibited in the prophetic hadith, and another that is praiseworthy and even required when done with proper Islamic etiquettes and piety, solely to reach the truth in subsidiary legal matters. The author clarifies that the prophetic prohibition only targets jadal for its own sake, not jadal that is necessary for defending the truth. This is supported by a quote from Ibn al-Athir, who relates that scholars interpret the relevant hadith as restricting the prohibition to theological debates about predestination and similar topics, while excluding debates about legal rulings which the Companions engaged in without prohibition.

Then the author moves to the practical application of praiseworthy jadal in the Sunnah, specifically in the Prophet's invitation of non-Muslims. The Prophet's practice shows that jadal for supporting the Islamic creed is not only allowed but exemplified, as he engaged in dialogue and debate with polytheists, Jews, and Christians as commanded by the Quran. The author details the Prophet's debate with the polytheists of Quraysh, describing how after the open call, they came to debate but were intellectually defeated, then resorted to mockery and persecution. A narrative from Ibn Hisham's Sira is cited, where the polytheists confront the Prophet listing their grievances about his teachings affecting their religion, unity, and ancestral traditions, and they offer him wealth and leadership in an attempt to bring him back. The lesson underscores that jadal is legitimate and praiseworthy when used for the sake of Allah and to convey the truth.

Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate
10 min
The Prophet's Jadal with the Polytheists: A Dialectical Model
Author narrates the Prophet's dialogue with polytheists as an example of jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a jadal exchange and distinguish between valid objections and irrelevant demands using the Prophet's example.

Core Idea
Jadal is not about winning but about conveying truth with patience and clear proof.

In this lesson, we examine a historical jadal between the Prophet and Quraysh. The polytheists employ various rhetorical strategies: offers of worldly gain, demands for miracles, and attempts to discredit the Prophet. The Prophet's responses demonstrate the proper method of da'wa: refusing to engage in worldly bargaining and consistently returning the focus to the message.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial context
Why did Quraysh initiate the debate? They were provoked by the Prophet's public denunciation of their idols and felt their status threatened.
2
List the polytheists' proposals
They offered wealth, honor, kingship, and medical help for supposed possession; then demanded miracles: moving mountains, rivers, resurrecting ancestors, sending an angel, and dropping a piece of the sky.
These are in order from the dialogue.
3
For each proposal, note the Prophet's response
He refused each offer, stating he was not sent for worldly gain. He consistently said his mission was only to convey the message, and acceptance is their benefit.
The Prophet's reasoning is consistent: his role is as a messenger, not a miracle-worker.
4
Classify each polytheist move
Are these valid objections? The offers are distractions; the demands for miracles are irrelevant (the Prophet's message is sufficient proof). Only the initial complaint about denouncing ancestors is a substantive objection.
Jadal rules: relevant arguments must address the claim, not change the subject.
5
Extract the key principle
The Prophet maintained focus on his message and did not let the debate drift. This teaches that in jadal, the defender must not be swayed by irrelevant demands or negotiations.

The Muslims, and explaining the rulings of creed and law to them and managing speech with them regarding that, perhaps they may be guided to the straight path. And this is what the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, followed in his call to the polytheists, and in his call to the Jews, and in his call to the Christians: A - The Prophet's debate with the polytheists: The Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, at the beginning of the call was keen to keep it secret, inclined to select those invited to it; but the matter did not remain so for him, for the command came from his Lord about the necessity of proclaiming the call and announcing it publicly, so he stood with what his Lord commanded him, calling to his Lord with excellence, and explaining to his people the creed of Islam in the best way. And in this public call there was what provoked Quraysh when the Prophet mentioned their deities with disfavor and explained the falsehood of worshiping them, so they came debating, but they were incapable of debate with the tongue, and they were a contentious people. So they resorted to mockery and ridicule, and incited the foolish against him, then the matter moved from debate and striking with proof to persecution and boycott of the Prophet, as we know from the prophetic biography. It is mentioned in the Sira of Ibn Hisham that when the polytheists were distressed with the Prophet and all their tricks had failed, they sent to him to speak with him and argue with him. He came to them, and they said: O Muhammad, we have sent for you to speak with you. By Allah, we do not know a man from the Arabs who brought to his people what you have brought to your people. You have reviled the fathers, disparaged the religion, insulted the gods, called the intellects foolish, and divided the community. There is no ugly thing left but you have brought it between us and you. If you have come with this talk seeking wealth, we will gather for you our wealth until you are the richest of us. If you are seeking honor among us, we will make you our chief. If you want kingship, we will make you king over us. And if this thing that comes to you is a vision you see that has overcome you, we will spend our wealth in seeking medicine for you until we cure you, or we will be excused concerning you. The Messenger of Allah said: There is none of what you say with me. I have not come with what I brought to you seeking your wealth, nor honor among you, nor kingship over you. But Allah sent me to you as a messenger, revealed to me His book, and commanded me to be to you a bringer of good tidings and a warner. I have conveyed to you the messages of my Lord and advised you. If you accept from me what I have brought to you, it is your fortune in this world and the hereafter. If you reject it, I will be patient for the command of Allah until Allah judges between me and you. Your station with Allah: They said: O Muhammad, if you will not accept anything we offered you, then you know that there is no one among the people with narrower territory, less water, and harsher life than us. So ask your Lord who sent you with what He sent you to move these mountains that have restricted us, and spread our land, and make rivers flow in it like the rivers of Syria and Iraq, and resurrect for us our forefathers from the past, and let among those resurrected be Qussay ibn Kilab, for he was a truthful elder, so we can ask them about what you say, whether it is true or false. If they confirm you, then we will believe you and know by that Allah and that He sent you as a messenger. He, prayers and peace be upon him, said: I was not sent to you with this. I have come to you with what I was sent with, and I have conveyed to you what I was sent with. If you accept it, it is your fortune in this world and the hereafter. If you reject it, I will be patient for the command of Allah until Allah judges between me and you. They said: If you do not do that, then ask your Lord to send with you an angel to confirm what you say and answer for you, and ask Him to give you gardens, palaces, and treasures of gold and silver to help you with, for we see you seeking livelihood, as you stand in the markets as we do and seek provision as we do, so that we may know your virtue and station with your Lord if you are a messenger as you claim. The Messenger of Allah said: I will not do that, nor will I ask my Lord for this. I was not sent with this, but Allah sent me as a bringer of good tidings and a warner. If you accept what I have brought, it is your fortune in this world and the hereafter. If you reject it, I will be patient until Allah judges between me and you. They said: Then drop on us pieces of the sky as you claim your Lord could do if He willed, for we will not believe you unless you do that. The Messenger of Allah said: That is up to Allah; if He wills, He will do it.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a historical narrative illustrating the Prophet's (SAW) method of jadal (dialectical argument) with the polytheists of Quraysh. It begins by describing the context: after the command for open preaching, the Prophet publicly denounced their idols, provoking them. They attempted debate but were unable to match his arguments, so they resorted to mockery and eventually persecution. The core of the lesson is the detailed dialogue from Ibn Hisham's Sira. The polytheists first try to negotiate by offering wealth, honor, and kingship, and even suggest the Prophet might be possessed, offering medical help. The Prophet's response is firm: he was not sent for worldly gain but as a messenger. They then demand miracles: moving mountains, rivers, and resurrecting ancestors. The Prophet refuses, stating his mission is only to convey the message. They then demand an angel and material rewards; again he refuses. Finally, they demand a punishment from the sky. The Prophet leaves it to Allah's will. Throughout, the Prophet maintains a calm, consistent response, emphasizing that acceptance of his message is their own benefit.

What was the Prophet's consistent response to the polytheists' demands for miracles?
A) He performed the miracles to convince them.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, the defender must stay focused on the core claim and not be sidetracked by irrelevant demands or worldly negotiations.

Dialectic -- Jadal

Blameworthy Jadal: Examples from Sira and Quran
Historical and Quranic examples of blameworthy jadal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the characteristics of blameworthy jadal (al-jadal al-madhmum) as exemplified in the Sira and Quran, and distinguish it from legitimate inquiry.

Core Idea
Blameworthy jadal seeks to defeat truth by demanding impossible proofs.

This passage from a classical usul work illustrates blameworthy jadal through historical and Quranic examples. The Quraysh's demands of the Prophet reveal the essence of jadal madhmum: shifting the burden of proof to unreasonable standards, refusing to accept clear evidence, and using arguments not to seek truth but to obstruct it. Understanding these examples helps you recognize when dialectical tactics cross into blameworthy territory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the demands of the Quraysh
List the key demands: kingship, moving mountains, resurrection of ancestors, an accompanying angel, treasures, dropping a piece of the sky. Notice the escalation from worldly offers to impossible natural transformations.
These demands are not about seeking evidence but about discrediting the Prophet.
2
Determine the Prophet's response
Observe that the Prophet consistently refuses to perform miracles on demand, reiterating that his mission is to convey Allah's message as a bearer of good news and a warner.
He does not engage in their game of setting impossible criteria.
3
Extract the logical pattern of jadal madhmum
Each demand sets an impossible standard that the opponent knows cannot be met, aiming to portray the claim as false because it does not meet those standards. This is a classic tactic of blameworthy dialectics.
The goal is to refute the truth, not to find it.
4
Connect to the Quranic examples
The polytheists also objected to the gradual revelation of the Quran, argued about predestination, and denied resurrection. These are also jadal madhmum because they demand explanations beyond their own limited reasoning or reject divine wisdom.
The Quran refutes each by divine declaration or rational explanation.
5
Formulate a definition of blameworthy jadal
Synthesize: Blameworthy jadal is disputation whose goal is to refute truth by demanding proofs that are either impossible or irrelevant to the claim, often involving evasion, obstinacy, or shifting burden of proof.
This contrasts with praiseworthy jadal, which seeks truth through sound reasoning.

كنت تريد به ملكا ملكناك علينا وإن كان هذا الذي يأتيك رئيا تراه قد غلب عليك بذلنا لك أموالنا في طلب الطب لك حتى نبرئك منه، أو نعذر فيك. فقال لهم الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم: ما بي ما تقولون، ما جئت بما جئتكم به أطلب أموالكم ولا الشرف فيكم ولا الملك عليكم، ولكن الله بعثني إليكم رسولا وأنزل علي كتابه وأمرني أن أكون لكم بشيرًا ونذيرا، فبلغتكم رسالات ربي، ونصحت لكم، فإن تقبلوا مني ما جئتكم به فهو حظكم في الدنيا والآخرة، وإن تردوه علي أصبر لأمر الله، حتى يحكم الله بيني وبينكم .

منزلتك من الله: قالوا يا محمد فإن كنت غير قابل منا شيئًا مما عرضنا عليك، فإنك قد علمت أنه ليس من الناس أحد أضيق بلدًا ولا أقل ماء ولا أشد عيشا منا، فسل لنا ربك الذي بعثك بما بعثك به فليسير عنا هذه الجبال التي قد ضيقت علينا، وليبسط لنا بلادنا وليفجر لنا فيها أنهارًا كأنهار الشام والعراق وليبعث لنا من مضى من آبائنا، وليكن فيمن يبعث لنا منهم قصي بن كلاب، فإنه كان شيخ صدق، فنسألهم عما تقول أحق هو أم باطل؟ فإن صدقوك صدقناك، وعرفنا به الله، وأنه بعثك رسولا كما تقول . فقال لهم صلوات الله وسلامه عليه ما بهذا بعثت إليكم، إنما جئتكم من بما بعثني به وقد بلغتكم ما أرسلت به إليكم فإن تقبلوه فهو حظكم من الدنيا والآخرة، وإن تردوه علي أصبر لأمر الله تعالى، حتى يحكم الله بيني وبينكم . قالوا : فإذا لم تفعل فسل ربك أن يبعث معك ملكا يصدقك بما تقول، ويراجعنا عنك، وسله فليجعل لك جنانًا وقصورًا وكنوزا من ذهب وفضة، يعينك بها عما نراك تبتغي فإنك تقوم في الأسواق كما نقوم، وتلتمس المعاش كما نلمسه حتى نعرف فضلك ومنزلتك عند ربك إن كنت رسولا كما تزعم . فقال لهم رسول الله : ما أنا بفاعل، وما أنا بالذي يسأل ربه هذا، وما بعثت إليكم بهذا، ولكن الله بعثني بشيرًا ونذيرا ، فإن تقبلوا ما جئتكم به فهو حظكم في الدنيا والآخرة، وإن تردوه علي أصبر حتى يحكم الله بيني وبينكم . قالوا فأسقط علينا كسفا من السماء كما زعمت أن ربك لو شاء فعل، فإنا لا نؤمن لك إلا أن تفعل، فقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : ذلك إلى الله إن شاء أن يفعله بكم فعل .

قالوا يا محمد أفما علم ربك أنا سنجلس معك ونسألك عما سألناك عنه، ونطلب منك ما نطلب فيتقدم إليك فيعلمك ما تراجعنا به، ويخبرك ما هو هو صانع في ذلك منا إذا لم تقبل منك ما جئتنا به، إنه قد بلغنا أنه يعلمك هذا رجل باليمامة يقال له الرحمن وإنا والله لا نؤمن بالرحمن أبدا، فقد أعذرنا إليك يا محمد، وإنا والله لا نتركك وما بلغت منا حتى نهلكك أو تهلكنا (1). وقد سجل القرآن الكريم الكثير من وقائع الجدل المذموم التي حاول المشركون من خلالها ثني النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام عن دعوته وتعجيزه عن الاستجابة لما يطلبونه منه لكي يثبت صدق نبوته قاصدين من ذلك دحض الحق وصرف الناس عنه .

فقد جادل المشركون رسول الله الله في نزول القرآن منجما (2) قائلين : لَوْلَا نزِلَ عَلَيْهِ الْقُرْآنُ جُمْلَةٌ وَاحِدَةً ، فبين الله لهم علة ذلك بقوله : كَذَلِكَ لِنُثَبِّتَ بِهِ فُؤَادَكَ وَرَتَلْتَهُ تَرتيلا ) (3) . وقد خاصم المشركون رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم القدر (4) ، فنزلت الآية: ﴿يَوْمَ يُسْحَبُونَ في النَّارِ عَلى وُجُوهِهِمْ ذُوقُوا مَسَّ سَقَرَ إِنَّا كُلَّ شَيْءٍ خَلَقْتَهُ بِقَدَرٍ وقد جادل المشركون في عقيدة البعث فأكثروا فيها الجدل، فإن عقليتهم لم تتسع إلى تصور الحياة بعد الموت، كما حكى القرآن على لسانهم : أَوذَا مِنْنَا وَكُنَّا (5).
"If you wanted kingship, we would make you king over us, and if this that comes to you is a vision you see that has overcome you, we would spend our wealth in seeking medicine for you until we cure you of it, or excuse ourselves regarding you." The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said to them: "I am not as you say. I have not come to you seeking your wealth, nor honor among you, nor kingship over you. But Allah sent me to you as a messenger, revealed to me His book, and commanded me to be a bearer of good news and a warner to you. So I have conveyed to you the messages of my Lord and advised you. If you accept from me what I have brought you, it is your share in this world and the Hereafter; if you reject it, I will be patient for the command of Allah until Allah judges between me and you."

"Your status with Allah?" They said: "O Muhammad, if you will not accept anything we offered you, then you know that there is no one with narrower land, less water, or harsher life than us. Ask your Lord who sent you to move these mountains that have constricted us, spread out our land, and cause rivers to flow like the rivers of Syria and Iraq. And raise up for us our forefathers, especially Qusayy ibn Kilab, for he was a truthful elder, so we can ask them whether what you say is true or false. If they affirm you, we will believe you and recognize Allah through them and that He sent you as a messenger." He, peace be upon him, said to them: "I was not sent to you for this. I have only come to you with what I was sent with, and I have conveyed what I was sent with to you. If you accept it, it is your share in this world and the Hereafter; if you reject it, I will be patient for the command of Allah until Allah judges between me and you." They said: "If you do not do that, ask your Lord to send an angel with you to confirm what you say and debate us on your behalf, and ask Him to make for you gardens, palaces, and treasures of gold and silver to support you, for we see you seeking a living in the markets as we do, until we know your merit and status with your Lord if you are a messenger as you claim." The Messenger of Allah said: "I will not do that, nor will I ask my Lord for this. I was not sent to you for this. But Allah sent me as a bearer of good news and a warner. If you accept what I have brought you, it is your share in this world and the Hereafter; if you reject it, I will be patient until Allah judges between me and you." They said: "Then drop on us a piece of the sky as you claim your Lord could do if He willed, for we will not believe you unless you do." The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: "That is up to Allah; if He wills to do it to you, He will."

They said: "O Muhammad, does your Lord not know that we will sit with you and ask you what we asked, and demand what we demand? Should He not inform you beforehand what to reply to us, and tell you what He will do with us if we do not accept what you brought us? Indeed, we have heard that a man in Yamama called al-Rahman teaches you this, and we will never believe in al-Rahman. So we have excused ourselves to you, O Muhammad, and we will not leave you and what you have brought to us until we destroy you or you destroy us." (1) The Noble Quran has recorded many instances of blameworthy jadal through which the polytheists tried to deter the Prophet, peace be upon him, from his call, and disable him from responding to what they demanded in order to prove the truth of his prophethood, intending by that to refute the truth and turn people away from it.

Indeed, the polytheists argued with the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, about the Quran being revealed gradually (2), saying: "Why was the Quran not revealed all at once?" So Allah explained the reason for that with His saying: "Thus, that We may strengthen thereby your heart, and We have recited it with a gradual recitation." (3) And the polytheists disputed with the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, about predestination (4), so the verse was revealed: "On the Day they are dragged into the Fire on their faces, they will be told: Taste the touch of Hellfire. Indeed, We have created everything according to a measure." And the polytheists argued about the doctrine of resurrection and engaged much in jadal about it, for their mentality could not comprehend life after death, as the Quran narrated from their tongues: "When we have died and become dust..." (5).
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a historical and quranic illustration of blameworthy jadal (الجدل المذموم). The author first narrates the exchange between the Quraysh polytheists and the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) from the Sira, showing how the polytheists made excessive and unreasonable demands to test his prophethood. They offered him worldly power and wealth, then asked for geological transformations, raising the dead, an accompanying angel, miraculous treasures, and even a piece of the sky to fall. Each demand was designed not to seek truth but to defeat the Prophet rhetorically and politically. The Prophet consistently refused, emphasizing that his mission was not to perform miracles on demand but to convey Allah's message. This illustrates the hallmark of blameworthy jadal: the opponent shifts the burden of proof to impossible standards. The author then cites Quranic examples: the polytheists objected to the gradual revelation of the Quran, argued about predestination, and denied resurrection. The Quran refutes each by divine declaration or rational explanation. The purpose of showing these examples is to define blameworthy jadal as that which seeks to undermine truth through specious objections rather than sincere inquiry. The author's method is to let the historical and revealed evidence speak for itself, demonstrating that jadal mazmum is not mere argumentation but a specific tactic of evasion and obstinacy.

Key Terms
الجدل المذموم
المشركون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Based on the passage, which of the following is NOT a characteristic of blameworthy jadal?
A) Demanding miracles that are not part of the prophet's mission.
B) Asking for rational explanations of revealed truths.
C) Rejecting clear evidence due to prejudice.
D) Shifting the topic to irrelevant demands.

Key Takeaway
Blameworthy jadal is characterized by demanding impossible proofs, rejecting clear evidence, and shifting the burden of proof to discredit truth, not to seek it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read the Sira exchange again. Identify three demands that are impossible to meet. Explain why each is an example of jadal madhmum.
?
The Quran says about the polytheists: 'And they say, "Why has this Quran not been sent down upon some great man from the two cities?"' (43:31). Is this an example of blameworthy jadal? Why or why not?

Dialectic -- Jadal
beginner
5 min
The Prophet's Jadal: From Argument to Escalation
Describes the Prophet's dialectical encounters with polytheists and their escalation to violence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the stages of dialectical encounter in the Prophet's interaction with the polytheists and explain why it turned to violence.

Core Idea
When rational argument fails, obstinate opponents may resort to violence — a pattern seen in the Prophet's jadal.

This lesson examines a historical example of jadal from the life of the Prophet. Notice how the polytheists first attempted to defeat him intellectually, and when they could not, they turned to harm. This pattern is important for understanding the nature of dialectic in Islamic tradition: it can be a prelude to conflict when the opponent rejects truth out of arrogance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the provocation
Al-As ibn Wa'il brings a decayed bone and mockingly asks if Allah can resurrect it. This is a challenge to the Prophet's teaching on resurrection.
The polytheist uses a physical object to argue by analogy – if the bone is decayed, how can it be revived?
2
Observe the Prophet's response
The Prophet affirms resurrection and warns the questioner of punishment, showing confidence in the divine promise.
He does not engage in a technical argument but asserts the truth directly and ties it to accountability.
3
Note the Quranic revelation
Verses from Surah Ya-Sin (36:77ff) are revealed, characterizing man as a clear adversary and reaffirming Allah's power to resurrect.
The Quran provides the ultimate proof, establishing the theological basis for the Prophet's claim.
4
Recognize the transition to violence
When the polytheists could not defeat the Prophet in argument, they replaced debate with harm, torture, and mockery.
This shows that jadal can escalate when the opponent is obstinate and refuses to accept rational proof.
5
Summarize the lesson
The pattern is clear: argument precedes persecution. Understanding this helps contextualize later Islamic discussions on the ethics of debate.
In formal jadal, the goal is truth; but historical practice shows that rejection can lead to conflict.

الجدل، حتى إن العاص بن وائل جاء بعظم بال إلى رسول الله، فتساءل ساخرا: إن كان الله يبعث ذلك العظم البالي؟ فقال رسول الله: نعم، يبعث الله هذا ثم يميتك ثم يحييك ثم يدخلك نار جهنم، فنزلت الآيات: ﴿أَوَلَمْ يَرَ الْإِنْسَانُ أَنَّا خَلَقْنَاهُ مِن نُّطْفَةٍ فَإِذَا هُوَ خَصِيمٌ مُبِينٌ﴾ إلى آخر السورة. وهكذا كانت نار الجدل اللفظي حامية الوطيس بين النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام وبين المشركين الذين بذلوا أقصى طاقتهم لتعجيزه وإظهار ضعفه، فلما عجزوا تحول الجدل إلى عنف متصاعد الأيام، وتم تعويض الجدل بالأذى والتعذيب والسخرية.
Dialectic, until al-As ibn Wa'il brought a decayed bone to the Messenger of Allah, asking mockingly: 'Will Allah resurrect this decayed bone?' The Messenger of Allah said: 'Yes, Allah will resurrect this, then He will cause you to die, then bring you to life, then enter you into the fire of Hell.' So the verses were revealed: 'Does not man see that We created him from a drop? Then he becomes a clear adversary' to the end of the surah. Thus the fire of verbal dialectic was blazing between the Prophet, peace be upon him, and the polytheists who exerted their utmost effort to incapacitate him and show his weakness. When they failed, dialectic turned into escalating violence, and dialectic was replaced by harm, torture, and mockery.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage describes the historical use of jadal (dialectic) by the Prophet Muhammad against the polytheists of Mecca. It begins with a specific anecdote: al-As ibn Wa'il brings a decayed bone to the Prophet and sarcastically asks if Allah can resurrect it. The Prophet affirms this and warns him of punishment, after which the Quranic verses from Surah Ya-Sin (36:77ff) were revealed. The author then generalizes that intense verbal dialectic took place between the Prophet and the polytheists, who tried to undermine his mission. When they failed to defeat him in argument, they resorted to physical violence, torture, and ridicule. This illustrates that jadal can be a prelude to conflict when the opponent refuses to accept rational proof. The passage is narrative, not analytical, but it establishes the early Islamic context of dialectic.

Key Terms
الجدل (al-jadal): Verbal dispute or argumentation, here referring to the Prophet's debates with polytheists using Quranic and rational proofs. [Note: In later Islamic logic, jadal becomes a formalized art (the fifth of the five arts) with strict rules, distinct from mere debate.]
المشركون (al-mushrikūn): Polytheists of Mecca who rejected the Prophet's message and engaged in dialectic before resorting to violence. [Note: Term stable throughout Islamic discourse referring to those who associate partners with Allah.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happened after the polytheists failed to defeat the Prophet in verbal dialectic?
A) They accepted Islam.
B) They resorted to violence, torture, and mockery.
C) They asked for more proofs.
D) They left Mecca.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's jadal with the polytheists illustrates that when a party is obstinate, dialectic can give way to physical oppression, a pattern with implications for how debate is conducted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the anecdote, what specific argument did the polytheist use to mock the Prophet's teaching? How did the Prophet respond, and what does this show about his dialectical method?
?
Compare this episode to a modern situation where debate turns to hostility. What are the common factors?

The Method of Jadal with Ahl al-Kitab
Describes the Prophet's dialectical exchanges with Jewish tribes using scripture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the Prophet's dialectical strategy for arguing with ahl al-kitab and identify the Quranic directive for gentleness.

Core Idea
Use the opponent's own scripture to refute their claims.

The Prophet's engagements with the Jews in Medina provide a model for interfaith argumentation rooted in scripture. This lesson unpacks the stages of that debate and the divine command to argue gently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three phases of interaction
Read the passage and note the shift from calm religious discussion to hostility, then to expulsion/war. This shows the ethical limit of jadal.
2
Extract the Quranic command for gentleness
Find the verse quoted: 'Do not argue with the People of the Scripture except in the best way' (29:46). Recognize this as the foundational rule for jadal.
3
Notice the Prophet's method of argumentation
The Prophet 'would bind them by what came in their books' – he used their own scriptures as the authoritative source to hold them accountable.
This is a key dialectical move: internal critique.
4
Recognize the shift from scriptural debate to action
When debate turned to insult and betrayal, the Prophet's method changed from argumentation to defensive measures.
Jadal has limits when the opponent is not acting in good faith.
