ب - جدل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم اليهود : لم يذكر كتاب السير شيئا من الاحتكاك الذي وقع بين النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وبين اليهود وهو بمكة المكرمة، حتى هاجر إلى المدينة المنورة فالتقى بهم، ودعاهم إلى دينه، ولكنهم أعرضوا ولاحوا النبي، وأسروا العداوة ونابذوه الشر، ولذلك اندفعوا لمجادلة النبي وسائر المسلمين وناقشوهم مناقشات دينية أخذت أولا طابعا دينيا هادئا ثم أخذت تظهر من جانبهم سبا واستهزاء وخيانة حتى اضطر النبي إلى إجلاء بعضهم ومحاربة الآخرين، وفي دور المجادلة كانت المجادلة واسعة والنطاق غير محدود لأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان يخاطب أقواما يقرون بكتاب ويؤمنون برسول، فالنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان يلزمهم بما جاء في كتبهم، وينعى عليهم مخالفتهم لما جاءت به رسلهم، وهم كانوا لعلمهم بالكتاب يوجهون أسئلة فيها شيء من الدقة والمعرفة وإن كانوا ضالين. وقد أمر الله نبيه صلى الله عليه وسلم أن يجادلهم برفق وحسن موعظة، فقال تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تُجَادِلُوا أَهْلَ الْكِتَابِ إِلَّا بِالَّتِي هِيَ أَحْسَنُ﴾، وقال تعالى: ﴿وَجَادِلْهُم بِالَّتِي هِيَ أَحْسَنُ﴾. وقد كانوا في مجادلتهم يدعون أن إبراهيم عليه السلام كان على ديانتهم، وقد رد الله عليهم تلك الدعوى في قوله تعالت كلماته: ﴿مَا كَانَ إِبْرَاهِيمُ يَهُودِيًّا وَلَا نَصْرَانِيًّا وَلَكِنْ كَانَ حَنِيفًا مُسْلِمًا وَمَا كَانَ مِنَ الْمُشْرِكِينَ﴾. وقد احتجوا على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بوجود النسخ في الشريعة الإسلامية؛ وأنكروا نسخ المعجزات والآيات، فَرَدَّ الله عليهم ذلك بقوله تعالى: ﴿مَا نَنسَخْ مِنْ آيَةٍ أَوْ نُنسِهَا نَأْتِ بِخَيْرٍ مِنْهَا أَوْ مِثْلِهَا أَلَمْ تَعْلَمْ أَنَّ اللَّهَ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ قَدِيرٌ﴾. وكانوا يطلبون آية أخرى تدل على رسالة النبي غير القرآن ويدعون أن تلك الآية: عهد من الله إليهم، لا يؤمنون بغيرها، وقد قال تعالى حاكيًا عنهم: ﴿الَّذِينَ قَالُوا إِنَّ اللهَ عَهِدَ إِلَيْنَا أَلَّا نُؤْمِنَ لِرَسُولٍ حَتَّى يَأْتِيَنَا بِقُرْبَانٍ تَأْكُلُهُ النَّارُ قُلْ قَدْ جَاءَكُمْ رُسُلٌ مِنْ قَبْلِي بِالْبَيِّنَاتِ وَبِالَّذِي قُلْتُمْ فَلِمَ قَتَلْتُمُوهُمْ إِنْ كُنْتُمْ صَادِقِينَ﴾. وطلبوا من النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أن ينزل عليهم كتابا من السماء يقرؤونه، وقد قال تعالى حكاية عنهم: ﴿يَسْأَلُكَ أَهْلُ الْكِتَابِ أَنْ تُنَزِّلَ عَلَيْهِمْ كِتَابًا مِنَ السَّمَاءِ فَقَدْ سَأَلُوا مُوسَى أَكْبَرَ مِنْ ذَلِكَ فَقَالُوا أَرِنَا اللَّهَ جَهْرَةً فَأَخَذَتْهُمُ الصَّاعِقَةُ بِظُلْمِهِمْ﴾. وكانوا يجادلون غير ذلك في أمور كثيرة من ذلك مثلا ما جاء في سيرة ابن هشام أن نفرا من أحبار يهود جاءوا رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم، فقالوا: يا محمد، أخبرنا عن أربع نسألك عنهن، فإن فعلت ذلك اتبعناك وصدقناك وآمنا بك، فقال لهم رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: عليكم بذلك عهد الله وميثاقه لئن أنا أخبرتكم بذلك لتصدقنني قالوا: نعم، قال: فاسألوا عما بدا لكم قالوا: فأخبرنا كيف يشبه الولد أمه، وإنما النطفة من الرجل؟ فقال لهم رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: أنشدكم بالله وبأيامه عند بني
B - The Prophet's dialectic with the Jews: The books of biographies do not mention any contact between the Prophet and the Jews while he was in Mecca, until he migrated to Medina and met them, called them to his religion, but they turned away and harassed the Prophet, harbored enmity and opposed him with evil. Therefore they rushed to argue with the Prophet and the rest of the Muslims and engaged in religious discussions that first took a calm religious character, then began to show from their side insult, mockery, and betrayal until the Prophet was forced to expel some of them and fight others. In the role of argumentation, the argumentation was wide and the scope unlimited because the Prophet addressed people who acknowledged a scripture and believed in a messenger. So the Prophet would bind them by what came in their books, and reproach them for contradicting what their messengers brought. And they, due to their knowledge of the scripture, would direct questions containing some precision and knowledge, though they were misguided. And Allah commanded His Prophet to argue with them gently and with good admonition, saying: 'Do not argue with the People of the Scripture except in the best way' and 'Argue with them in the best way.' They claimed in their argument that Abraham was upon their religion, and Allah refuted that claim in His words: 'Abraham was neither a Jew nor a Christian, but he was a hanif, a Muslim, and not of the polytheists.' They argued against the Prophet concerning abrogation in Islamic law, denying the abrogation of miracles and verses, so Allah refuted them with His words: 'Whatever verse We abrogate or cause to be forgotten, We bring a better one or similar to it. Do you not know that Allah is over all things competent?' They asked for another sign indicating the Prophet's message besides the Quran, claiming that sign was a covenant from Allah to them, they would not believe otherwise. Allah narrated: 'Those who said, “Indeed Allah has taken a covenant from us that we will not believe in a messenger until he brings us an offering consumed by fire.” Say, 'There have come to you messengers before me with clear proofs and with that which you said; so why did you kill them, if you are truthful?' And they asked the Prophet to send down to them a book from heaven they could read. Allah narrated: 'The People of the Scripture ask you to bring down to them a book from heaven. They asked Moses for greater than that and said, 'Show us Allah outwardly,' so the thunderbolt seized them for their wrongdoing.' And they argued about many other things, for example what is in the Sira of Ibn Hisham: a group of Jewish rabbis came to the Messenger of Allah and said, 'O Muhammad, inform us about four things we ask you; if you do that we will follow you, believe you, and have faith in you.' The Messenger of Allah said, 'You have the covenant and pledge of Allah for that; if I inform you of that, you will surely believe me.' They said, 'Yes.' He said, 'Then ask what you wish.' They said, 'Tell us: how does a child resemble his mother, when the semen is from the man?' The Messenger of Allah said, 'I adjure you by Allah and by His days among the Banu...'
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage details the Prophet's dialectical engagements with the Jewish tribes of Medina. It begins with historical context: no significant contact occurred in Mecca, but after the Hijra, Jews initially debated peacefully but later resorted to insults and betrayal, leading to their expulsion or war. The author emphasizes that the Prophet's method was to argue from their own scriptures, holding them accountable to their own books. He would reproach them for contradicting their prophets. Allah commanded gentleness in this dialectic (Quran 29:46, 16:125). Specific topics of their arguments are listed: (1) They claimed Abraham followed their religion; Allah refuted via Quran 3:67. (2) They objected to abrogation in Islamic law; Allah responded with Quran 2:106. (3) They demanded a specific sign (a fire-consuming offering); Quran 3:183 refutes this. (4) They asked for a book from heaven; Quran 4:153 reminds them of Moses' request and its consequence. Finally, a narrated incident from Ibn Hisham's Sira shows Jewish rabbis posing four questions to test the Prophet, including a scientific question about embryology. The passage demonstrates that jadal with People of the Book was conducted with respect and relied on scriptural authority.

B) ﴿مَا نَنسَخْ مِنْ آيَةٍ أَوْ نُنسِهَا نَأْتِ بِخَيْرٍ مِنْهَا أَوْ مِثْلِهَا﴾
C) ﴿مَا كَانَ إِبْرَاهِيمُ يَهُودِيًّا وَلَا نَصْرَانِيًّا﴾
D) ﴿يَسْأَلُكَ أَهْلُ الْكِتَابِ أَنْ تُنَزِّلَ عَلَيْهِمْ كِتَابًا﴾

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's jadal with the Jews was based on gentleness and using their own scriptures as the authoritative reference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a brief dialectical exchange between a Muslim and a Christian using the method of arguing from the Christian's own Bible. For example, use Matthew 5:17 ('I came not to abolish the Law and the Prophets') to argue that Jesus did not abrogate previous laws, analogous to the Prophet's use of Jewish scriptures.
?
Identify a modern situation where the Prophet's method of gentleness and scriptural appeal could be applied, and explain why it would be effective.

Dialectic -- Jadal
advanced
10 min
Refuting Jewish Arguments with Scriptural Proofs
Lists Jewish objections and Quranic answers in the Prophet's dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyse how the Quran refutes specific claims of the Jews using their own premises and apply this scriptural reasoning method.

Core Idea
Each Jewish objection is met with a Quranic verse that uses their own logic.

The Jews of Medina raised several arguments against the Prophet: about Abraham's religion, abrogation, a fire-consuming offering, and a heavenly book. The Quran refutes each by appealing to what they already accept. This lesson dissects those refutations.

Guided Path
1
List the four Jewish arguments
From the passage, identify: (1) Abraham was a Jew, (2) abrogation invalidates Islam, (3) demand for a specific sign (fire-consuming offering), (4) demand for a book from heaven.
2
Locate the Quranic refutations
For each argument, find the corresponding verse quoted in the passage: Quran 3:67, 2:106, 3:183, 4:153.
3
Analyse the logical structure of each refutation
Check if the refutation uses the Jews' own premises. For example, the refutation of the abrogation argument uses the premise that God is all-powerful (2:106). The refutation of the fire-offering argument points out that they killed previous messengers who brought such signs (3:183).
This is a reductio ad absurdum: if you reject me for not producing this sign, you rejected earlier prophets and killed them, showing inconsistency.
4
Evaluate the strength of each refutation
Consider whether the refutation is logically sound and whether it would be convincing to the Jews. Note that the Jews are portrayed as obstinate, so even sound arguments did not convince them.
The strength lies in using their own accepted texts; the weakness is their prior commitment to disbelief.

دينه، ولكنهم أعرضوا ولاحوا النبي، وأسروا العداوة ونابذوه الشر، ولذلك اندفعوا لمجادلة النبي وسائر المسلمين وناقشوهم مناقشات دينية أخذت أولا طابعا دينيا هادئا ثم أخذت تظهر من جانبهم سبا واستهزاء وخيانة الْمُشْرِكِينَ﴾. وقد احتجوا على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بوجود النسخ في الشريعة الإسلامية؛ وأنكروا نسخ المعجزات والآيات، فَرَدَّ الله عليهم ذلك بقوله تعالى: ﴿مَا نَنسَخْ مِنْ آيَةٍ أَوْ نُنسِهَا نَأْتِ بِخَيْرٍ مِنْهَا أَوْ مِثْلِهَا أَلَمْ تَعْلَمْ أَنَّ اللَّهَ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ قَدِيرٌ﴾. وكانوا يطلبون آية أخرى تدل على رسالة النبي غير القرآن ويدعون أن تلك الآية: عهد من الله إليهم، لا Dialectic -- Jadal

The Prophet's Dialectical Method with the People of the Book
Exposition of the Prophet's dialectical method with Jews and Christians, with Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two key elements of the Prophet's jadal method: appealing to common scripture and using authoritative oaths.

Core Idea
Prophetic jadal establishes truth by appealing to what the opponent already knows.

In this passage, the author illustrates how the Prophet engaged in dialectical argumentation with Jews and Christians, relying on Quranic commands to dispute in the best manner. The core technique was to invoke their own revealed knowledge and scriptures, using solemn oaths to affirm truths they could not deny. This lesson unpacks that method and shows how it can be applied in theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic mandate for jadal
The passage begins with Quranic verses commanding argumentation with the People of the Book in a better way. Note the verse references (29:46, 16:125) and their implications.
These verses set the ethical framework for dialectic in Islam.
2
Extract the method of appealing to common ground
In the four questions encounter, the Prophet repeatedly says 'I adjure you by God and by His days among the Children of Israel, do you know that...' This shows he uses their own scriptures as a basis for argument.
This is a form of argument from authority accepted by both sides.
3
Observe the response pattern of the opponents
The Jews admitted the truth of the Prophet's answers but still rejected him, revealing that their objection was not based on ignorance but obstinacy.
This highlights the difference between genuine inquiry and stubborn disputation.
4
Contrast with the Prophetic response
The Prophet maintained gentleness and firmness, not resorting to harshness even when faced with recalcitrance.
The balance of leniency and resoluteness is a key lesson for dialecticians.

الجدل

تُحَدِلُوا أَهْلَ الكِتب إِلَّا بِالَّتِي هِيَ أَحْسَنُ، وقال تعالى: ﴿وَحَدِلْهُم بِالَّتِي هِيَ أَحْسَنُ﴾، وقد كانوا في مجادلتهم يدعون أن إبراهيم عليه السلام كان على ديانتهم، وقد رد الله عليهم تلك الدعوى في قوله تعالت كلماته: ﴿مَا كَانَ إبراهيم يهوديا ولا نصرانيا ولاكن كان حَنِيفًا مُسْلِمًا وَمَا كَانَ مِنَ الْمُشْرِكِينَ﴾ وقد احتجوا على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بوجود النسخ في الشريعة الإسلامية؛ وأنكروا نسخ المعجزات والآيات، فَرَدَّ الله عليهم ذلك بقوله تعالى: ﴿مَا نَنسَحْ مِنْ ءَايَةٍ أَوْ نُنسِهَا نَأْتِ بِخَيْرٍ مِنْهَا أَوْ مِثْلِهَا أَلَمْ تَعْلَمْ أَنَّ اللَّهَ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ قَدِيرٌ﴾، وكانوا يطلبون آية أخرى تدل على رسالة النبي غير القرآن ويدعون أن تلك الآية: عهد من الله إليهم، لا يؤمنون بغيرها، وقد قال تعالى حاكيًا عنهم: ﴿الَّذِينَ قَالُوا إِنَّ اللهَ عَهِدَ إِلَيْنَا أَلَّا نُؤْمِنَ لِرَسُولٍ حَتَّى يَأْتِيَنَا بِقُرَبَانِ تَأْكُلُهُ النَّارُ قُلْ قَدْ جَاءَكُمْ رُسُلٌ مِّن قَبْلِي بِالْبَيِّنَتِ وَبِالَّذِى قُلْتُمْ فَلِمَ قَتَلْتُمُوهُمْ إِن كُنتُمْ صَدِقِينَ﴾، وطلبوا من النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أن ينزل عليهم كتابا السماء يقرؤونه، وقد قال تعالى حكاية عنهم: ﴿يَسْتَلْكَ أَهْلُ الْكِتَابِ أَن تُنَزِلَ عَلَيْهِمْ كِتَبا مِّنَ السَّمَاءِ فَقَدْ سَأَلُوا مُوسَى أَكْبَرَ مِن ذَلِكَ فَقَالُوا أَرِنَا اللَّهَ جَهْرَةً فَأَخَذَتْهُمُ الصَّعِقَةُ بِظُلْمِهِم﴾

وكانوا يجادلون غير ذلك في أمور كثيرة من ذلك مثلا ما جاء في سيرة ابن هشام أن نفرا من أحبار يهود جاءوا رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ، فقالوا: يا محمد، أخبرنا عن أربع نسألك عنهن، فإن فعلت ذلك اتبعناك وصدقناك وآمنا بك، فقال لهم رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: عليكم بذلك عهد الله وميثاقه لئن أنا أخبرتكم بدلك لتصدقنني قالوا: نعم، قال: فاسألوا عما بدا لكم قالوا فأخبرنا كيف يشبه الولد أمه، وإنما النطفة من الرجل؟ فقال لهم رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: أنشدكم بالله وبأيامه عند بني إسرائيل، هل تعلمون أن نطفة الرجل بيضاء غليظة، ونطفة المرأة صفراء رقيقة، فأيتها غلبت صاحبتها كان لها الشبه قالوا اللهم نعم، قالوا: فأخبرنا كيف نومك؟ فقال، أنشدكم بالله وبأيامه عند بني إسرائيل، هل تعلمون أن نوم الذي تزعمون أني لست به تنام عينه وقلبه يقظان؟ فقالوا: اللهم نعم، قال: فكذلك نومي، تنام عيني وقلبي يقظان قالوا فأخبرنا عما حرم إسرائيل على نفسه؟ قال: أنشدكم بالله وبأيامه عند بني إسرائيل هل تعلمون أنه كان أحب الطعام والشراب إليه ألبان الإبل، ولحومها وأنه اشتكى شكوى فعافاه الله منها، فحرم على نفسه أحب الطعام والشراب إليه شكرًا لله، قالوا: اللهم نعم، قالوا: فأخبرنا عن الروح، قال: أنشدكم بالله وبأيامه عند بني إسرائيل هل تعلمونه جبريل، وهو للذي يأتيني؟ قالوا: اللهم نعم، ولكنه يا محمد لنا عدو، وهو ملك إنما يأتي بالشدة وبسفك الدماء، ولولا ذلك لا تبعناك، فأنزل الله عز وجل فيهم: ﴿قُلْ من كانَ عَدُوًّا لِجِبْرِيلَ فَإِنَّهُ نَزَّلَهُ عَلَى قَلْبِكَ بِإِذْنِ اللهِ مُصَدِّقًا لِمَا بَيْنَ يَدَيْهِ وَهُدًى وَبُشْرَى لِلْمُؤْمِنِينَ مَن كَانَ عَدُوًّا لِلَّهِ وَمَلَتَبِكَتِهِ، وَرُسُلِهِ، وَجِبْرِيلَ وَمِيكَنلَ فَإِنَّ اللهَ عَدُوٌّ لِلْكَفِرِينَ وَلَقَدْ أَنزَلْنَا إِلَيْكَ وَايَتِ بَيْنَتٍ وَمَا يَكْفُرُ بِهَا إِلَّا الْفَاسِقُونَ أَوَكُلَّمَا عَهَدُوا عَهْدًا نَبَذَهُ فَرِيقٌ مِنْهُمْ بَلْ أَكْثَرُهُمْ لَا يُؤْمِنُونَ﴾ وترى من هذا أن جدلهم مع النبي لو كان كجدل أسلافهم مع موسى عليه السلام، جدل المتعنتين الذين لا يطلبون رشادًا ولا يبغون سدادا ولا يريدون حقًا ينصرونه، بل باطلا يلوون ألسنتهم به، ومع ذلك فالنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان يأخذهم برفق وعطف وأناة حينا، وحزم حينا.

جدل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم مع النصارى: وأما جدله عليه الصلاة والسلام مع النصارى فقد كان قليلا، لبعدهم عنه، وعدم اختلاطهم بالمسلمين إلا قليلا، وكان النبي في جدله معهم يهاجمهم في عقيدة التثليث، ويبين كفرهم بها، كما قال تعالى: ﴿لَقَدْ كَفَرَ الَّذِينَ قَالُوا إِنَّ﴾
Argumentation (jadal). "Dispute with the People of the Scripture except in a way that is better..." And He said: "And argue with them in a way that is better." And they used to claim in their disputation that Abraham was upon their religion, and God refuted that claim in His words: "Abraham was neither a Jew nor a Christian, but he was a true Muslim (Hanif) and was not of the polytheists." And they argued against the Prophet (peace be upon him) about the existence of abrogation in Islamic law, and they denied the abrogation of miracles and verses, so God refuted that with His words: "We do not abrogate a verse or cause it to be forgotten except that We bring something better or similar. Do you not know that God is over all things competent?" And they asked for another sign indicating the Prophet's message other than the Quran, claiming that that sign was a covenant from God to them, they would not believe in any other. And God said, relating from them: "Those who said, 'Indeed, God has taken a covenant from us not to believe in any messenger until he brings us an offering consumed by fire.' Say, 'There have come to you messengers before me with clear proofs and with that which you said, so why did you kill them, if you are truthful?'" And they asked the Prophet to send down to them a book from the heaven that they could read. And God said, relating from them: "The People of the Scripture ask you to bring down to them a book from the heaven. But they had asked Moses for what was greater than that, and said, 'Show us God clearly,' so the thunderbolt took them for their wrongdoing."

And they used to argue about many other things, such as what is narrated in the Sira of Ibn Hisham: A group of Jewish rabbis came to the Messenger of God and said: "O Muhammad, inform us about four things we will ask you about. If you do that, we will follow you, believe you, and have faith in you." The Messenger of God said: "You have the covenant of God and His pledge that if I inform you, you will believe me." They said: "Yes." He said: "Ask what you will." They said: "Tell us how the child resembles its mother, even though the sperm comes from the man?" The Messenger of God said: "I adjure you by God and by His days among the Children of Israel, do you know that the man's sperm is white and thick, and the woman's fluid is yellow and thin, and whichever of them overcomes the other, the child resembles that one?" They said: "O God, yes." They said: "Tell us about your sleep." He said: "I adjure you by God and by His days among the Children of Israel, do you know that the sleep of the one you claim I am not (i.e., the true prophet) has his eye sleeping but his heart awake?" They said: "O God, yes." He said: "Thus is my sleep; my eye sleeps but my heart is awake." They said: "Tell us about what Israel (Jacob) forbade for himself." He said: "I adjure you by God and by His days among the Children of Israel, do you know that the most beloved food and drink to him were camel milk and meat, and he suffered an illness from which God cured him, so he forbade for himself the most beloved food and drink to him as thanks to God?" They said: "O God, yes." They said: "Tell us about the Spirit (Gabriel)." He said: "I adjure you by God and by His days among the Children of Israel, do you know that it is Gabriel who comes to me?" They said: "O God, yes, but he is our enemy, O Muhammad; he is an angel who only brings hardship and bloodshed, and if not for that, we would have followed you." So God revealed concerning them: "Say, 'Whoever is an enemy to Gabriel – for indeed he brought it down upon your heart by permission of God, confirming what was before it, and as guidance and good tidings for the believers. Whoever is an enemy to God, His angels, His messengers, Gabriel, and Michael – then indeed God is an enemy to the disbelievers.' And We have certainly sent down to you clear verses, and none disbelieve in them except the defiantly disobedient. Is it not [the case] that whenever they make a covenant, a party of them throws it aside? But most of them do not believe." And you see from this that their argument with the Prophet was like the argument of their forefathers with Moses, the argument of obstinate people who do not seek guidance nor desire correctness nor want a truth to support, but rather falsehood, twisting their tongues with it, yet despite that, the Prophet would treat them with gentleness, kindness, and deliberation at times, and firmness at other times.

The Prophet's argument with the Christians: As for his argument with the Christians, it was little, due to their distance from him and their lack of mixing with Muslims except rarely, and the Prophet in his argument with them would attack the doctrine of the Trinity and show their disbelief in it, as God said: "They have certainly disbelieved who say, 'Indeed...'"
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a historical and scriptural exposition of the dialectical method (jadal) employed by the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) in his engagements with the People of the Book, i.e., Jews and Christians. The author begins by grounding the practice of dialectic in Quranic injunctions, citing verses that command disputing with the People of the Book in a manner that is best (Quran 29:46 and 16:125). The author then recounts several instances of Jewish arguments against the Prophet: they claimed Abraham as their own, but the Quran refutes this by stating Abraham was a hanif (monotheist) not a Jew or Christian. They objected to abrogation (naskh) in Islamic law, and the Quran responded that abrogation is God's prerogative and He brings something better. They demanded a sign other than the Quran, claiming a covenant from God requiring a fire-consumed offering, and the Quran exposed their inconsistency by noting they had killed previous messengers despite such signs. They asked the Prophet to bring down a book from heaven, and the Quran compares this to the Israelites' demand to see God, which led to their destruction.

The author then narrates a detailed encounter from Ibn Hisham's Sira, where Jewish rabbis posed four questions to test the Prophet's prophethood. The Prophet responded by invoking their own scriptures and knowledge, using a method of swearing by God and asking them to affirm truths known to them. This illustrates the dialectical technique of appealing to common ground and accepted knowledge (ilm) to establish credibility. The questions cover embryology, the nature of prophetic sleep, the self-imposed prohibition of Jacob, and the identity of the archangel Gabriel. When the rabbis admitted the truth of his answers but rejected him due to enmity to Gabriel, the Quran was revealed condemning their enmity. The author concludes that their jadal was that of obstinate people (mut'annitin) who seek not truth but falsehood, yet the Prophet responded with a mix of gentleness and firmness.

Finally, the author notes that the Prophet's jadal with Christians was limited, focusing on refuting the doctrine of the Trinity, citing the Quranic verse that declares disbelief in those who say God is one of three. The passage thus serves both as a historical account and a model for proper dialectical engagement: relying on revelation, appealing to common ground, and balancing softness with resoluteness.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the Prophet's approach when the Jewish rabbis posed challenging questions?
A) He immediately refuted them with harsh words.
B) He invoked their own scripture and knowledge, swearing by God to affirm common truths.
C) He ignored their questions and changed the subject.
D) He performed a miracle to silence them.

Key Takeaway
Prophetic jadal relies on common scripture and solemn oaths to establish truths that the opponent cannot deny.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Using the Quranic verses cited in this passage, reconstruct the Prophet's argument against the Jews' claim that Abraham was Jewish or Christian. What form does this argument take (e.g., appeal to authority, reductio)?
?
2. In a hypothetical debate with a Christian about the Trinity, design a response that follows the Prophetic method: appeal to their scripture and use an oath to affirm a common truth.

Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate
6 min
Obstinate Disputation (Muta'annit) and the Prophetic Response
Exposition of the Prophet's dialectical method with Jews and Christians, with Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between truth-seeking jadal and obstinate disputation (muta'annit) using criteria from the passage.

Core Idea
The Prophet faced obstinate disputants who twisted truth, yet he responded with measured resolve.

The passage concludes by characterizing the Jewish disputants as muta'annit – stubborn arguers who do not seek guidance. Yet the Prophet did not abandon the method of dialectic. This lesson helps you identify the signs of obstinate disputation and the proper Prophetic response: a combination of gentleness and firmness.

Guided Path
1
Define 'muta'annit' from the passage
Find the sentence: 'جدل المتعنتين الذين لا يطلبون رشادًا ولا يبغون سدادا ولا يريدون حقًا ينصرونه'. Translate and analyze its components: they do not seek guidance, do not desire correctness, do not want truth.
This definition is central to understanding the difference between constructive and destructive debate.
2
Identify obstinate behavior in the examples
The Jews admitted the truth of the Prophet's answers but still rejected him. They demanded signs despite clear miracles. They twisted the issue of Gabriel. These are signs of obstinacy.
Obstinacy is not ignorance but a willful rejection of truth after it is recognized.
3
Analyze the Prophetic response: balance of leniency and firmness
The passage says the Prophet treated them with gentleness, kindness, and deliberation at times, and firmness at other times. Note that he did not abandon debate even when faced with obstinacy.
This balance is a key lesson for anyone engaged in theological debate.
4
Apply the distinction to a modern Kalam debate
Consider a contemporary debate on the attributes of God. An opponent who admits textual evidence but refuses to accept conclusions due to emotional reasons is exhibiting muta'annit behavior. How would you respond?
The Prophetic model advises persistence in truth with appropriate demeanor.

الجدل

تُحَدِلُوا أَهْلَ الكِتب إِلَّا بِالَّتِي هِيَ أَحْسَنُ، وقال تعالى: ﴿وَحَدِلْهُم بِالَّتِي هِيَ أَحْسَنُ﴾، وقد كانوا في مجادلتهم يدعون أن إبراهيم عليه السلام كان على ديانتهم، وقد رد الله عليهم تلك الدعوى في قوله تعالت كلماته: ﴿مَا كَانَ إبراهيم يهوديا ولا نصرانيا ولاكن كان حَنِيفًا مُسْلِمًا وَمَا كَانَ مِنَ الْمُشْرِكِينَ﴾ وقد احتجوا على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بوجود النسخ في الشريعة الإسلامية؛ وأنكروا نسخ المعجزات والآيات، فَرَدَّ الله عليهم ذلك بقوله تعالى: ﴿مَا نَنسَحْ مِنْ ءَايَةٍ أَوْ نُنسِهَا نَأْتِ بِخَيْرٍ مِنْهَا أَوْ مِثْلِهَا أَلَمْ تَعْلَمْ أَنَّ اللَّهَ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ قَدِيرٌ﴾، وكانوا يطلبون آية أخرى تدل على رسالة النبي غير القرآن ويدعون أن تلك الآية: عهد من الله إليهم، لا يؤمنون بغيرها، وقد قال تعالى حاكيًا عنهم: ﴿الَّذِينَ قَالُوا إِنَّ اللهَ عَهِدَ إِلَيْنَا أَلَّا نُؤْمِنَ لِرَسُولٍ حَتَّى يَأْتِيَنَا بِقُرَبَانِ تَأْكُلُهُ النَّارُ قُلْ قَدْ جَاءَكُمْ رُسُلٌ مِّن قَبْلِي بِالْبَيِّنَتِ وَبِالَّذِى قُلْتُمْ فَلِمَ قَتَلْتُمُوهُمْ إِن كُنتُمْ صَدِقِينَ﴾، وطلبوا من النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أن ينزل عليهم كتابا السماء يقرؤونه، وقد قال تعالى حكاية عنهم: ﴿يَسْتَلْكَ أَهْلُ الْكِتَابِ أَن تُنَزِلَ عَلَيْهِمْ كِتَبا مِّنَ السَّمَاءِ فَقَدْ سَأَلُوا مُوسَى أَكْبَرَ مِن ذَلِكَ فَقَالُوا أَرِنَا اللَّهَ جَهْرَةً فَأَخَذَتْهُمُ الصَّعِقَةُ بِظُلْمِهِم﴾

وكانوا يجادلون غير ذلك في أمور كثيرة من ذلك مثلا ما جاء في سيرة ابن هشام أن نفرا من أحبار يهود جاءوا رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ، فقالوا: يا محمد، أخبرنا عن أربع نسألك عنهن، فإن فعلت ذلك اتبعناك وصدقناك وآمنا بك، فقال لهم رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: عليكم بذلك عهد الله وميثاقه لئن أنا أخبرتكم بدلك لتصدقنني قالوا: نعم، قال: فاسألوا عما بدا لكم قالوا فأخبرنا كيف يشبه الولد أمه، وإنما النطفة من الرجل؟ فقال لهم رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: أنشدكم بالله وبأيامه عند بني إسرائيل، هل تعلمون أن نطفة الرجل بيضاء غليظة، ونطفة المرأة صفراء رقيقة، فأيتها غلبت صاحبتها كان لها الشبه قالوا اللهم نعم، قالوا: فأخبرنا كيف نومك؟ فقال، أنشدكم بالله وبأيامه عند بني إسرائيل، هل تعلمون أن نوم الذي تزعمون أني لست به تنام عينه وقلبه يقظان؟ فقالوا: اللهم نعم، قال: فكذلك نومي، تنام عيني وقلبي يقظان قالوا فأخبرنا عما حرم إسرائيل على نفسه؟ قال: أنشدكم بالله وبأيامه عند بني إسرائيل هل تعلمون أنه كان أحب الطعام والشراب إليه ألبان الإبل، ولحومها وأنه اشتكى شكوى فعافاه الله منها، فحرم على نفسه أحب الطعام والشراب إليه شكرًا لله، قالوا: اللهم نعم، قالوا: فأخبرنا عن الروح، قال: أنشدكم بالله وبأيامه عند بني إسرائيل هل تعلمونه جبريل، وهو للذي يأتيني؟ قالوا: اللهم نعم، ولكنه يا محمد لنا عدو، وهو ملك إنما يأتي بالشدة وبسفك الدماء، ولولا ذلك لا تبعناك، فأنزل الله عز وجل فيهم: ﴿قُلْ من كانَ عَدُوًّا لِجِبْرِيلَ فَإِنَّهُ نَزَّلَهُ عَلَى قَلْبِكَ بِإِذْنِ اللهِ مُصَدِّقًا لِمَا بَيْنَ يَدَيْهِ وَهُدًى وَبُشْرَى لِلْمُؤْمِنِينَ مَن كَانَ عَدُوًّا لِلَّهِ وَمَلَتَبِكَتِهِ، وَرُسُلِهِ، وَجِبْرِيلَ وَمِيكَنلَ فَإِنَّ اللهَ عَدُوٌّ لِلْكَفِرِينَ وَلَقَدْ أَنزَلْنَا إِلَيْكَ وَايَتِ بَيْنَتٍ وَمَا يَكْفُرُ بِهَا إِلَّا الْفَاسِقُونَ أَوَكُلَّمَا عَهَدُوا عَهْدًا نَبَذَهُ فَرِيقٌ مِنْهُمْ بَلْ أَكْثَرُهُمْ لَا يُؤْمِنُونَ﴾ وترى من هذا أن جدلهم مع النبي لو كان كجدل أسلافهم مع موسى عليه السلام، جدل المتعنتين الذين لا يطلبون رشادًا ولا يبغون سدادا ولا يريدون حقًا ينصرونه، بل باطلا يلوون ألسنتهم به، ومع ذلك فالنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان يأخذهم برفق وعطف وأناة حينا، وحزم حينا.

جدل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم مع النصارى: وأما جدله عليه الصلاة والسلام مع النصارى فقد كان قليلا، لبعدهم عنه، وعدم اختلاطهم بالمسلمين إلا قليلا، وكان النبي في جدله معهم يهاجمهم في عقيدة التثليث، ويبين كفرهم بها، كما قال تعالى: ﴿لَقَدْ كَفَرَ الَّذِينَ قَالُوا إِنَّ﴾
Argumentation (jadal). "Dispute with the People of the Scripture except in a way that is better..." And He said: "And argue with them in a way that is better." And they used to claim in their disputation that Abraham was upon their religion, and God refuted that claim in His words: "Abraham was neither a Jew nor a Christian, but he was a true Muslim (Hanif) and was not of the polytheists." And they argued against the Prophet (peace be upon him) about the existence of abrogation in Islamic law, and they denied the abrogation of miracles and verses, so God refuted that with His words: "We do not abrogate a verse or cause it to be forgotten except that We bring something better or similar. Do you not know that God is over all things competent?" And they asked for another sign indicating the Prophet's message other than the Quran, claiming that that sign was a covenant from God to them, they would not believe in any other. And God said, relating from them: "Those who said, 'Indeed, God has taken a covenant from us not to believe in any messenger until he brings us an offering consumed by fire.' Say, 'There have come to you messengers before me with clear proofs and with that which you said, so why did you kill them, if you are truthful?'" And they asked the Prophet to send down to them a book from the heaven that they could read. And God said, relating from them: "The People of the Scripture ask you to bring down to them a book from the heaven. But they had asked Moses for what was greater than that, and said, 'Show us God clearly,' so the thunderbolt took them for their wrongdoing."

And they used to argue about many other things, such as what is narrated in the Sira of Ibn Hisham: A group of Jewish rabbis came to the Messenger of God and said: "O Muhammad, inform us about four things we will ask you about. If you do that, we will follow you, believe you, and have faith in you." The Messenger of God said: "You have the covenant of God and His pledge that if I inform you, you will believe me." They said: "Yes." He said: "Ask what you will." They said: "Tell us how the child resembles its mother, even though the sperm comes from the man?" The Messenger of God said: "I adjure you by God and by His days among the Children of Israel, do you know that the man's sperm is white and thick, and the woman's fluid is yellow and thin, and whichever of them overcomes the other, the child resembles that one?" They said: "O God, yes." They said: "Tell us about your sleep." He said: "I adjure you by God and by His days among the Children of Israel, do you know that the sleep of the one you claim I am not (i.e., the true prophet) has his eye sleeping but his heart awake?" They said: "O God, yes." He said: "Thus is my sleep; my eye sleeps but my heart is awake." They said: "Tell us about what Israel (Jacob) forbade for himself." He said: "I adjure you by God and by His days among the Children of Israel, do you know that the most beloved food and drink to him were camel milk and meat, and he suffered an illness from which God cured him, so he forbade for himself the most beloved food and drink to him as thanks to God?" They said: "O God, yes." They said: "Tell us about the Spirit (Gabriel)." He said: "I adjure you by God and by His days among the Children of Israel, do you know that it is Gabriel who comes to me?" They said: "O God, yes, but he is our enemy, O Muhammad; he is an angel who only brings hardship and bloodshed, and if not for that, we would have followed you." So God revealed concerning them: "Say, 'Whoever is an enemy to Gabriel – for indeed he brought it down upon your heart by permission of God, confirming what was before it, and as guidance and good tidings for the believers. Whoever is an enemy to God, His angels, His messengers, Gabriel, and Michael – then indeed God is an enemy to the disbelievers.' And We have certainly sent down to you clear verses, and none disbelieve in them except the defiantly disobedient. Is it not [the case] that whenever they make a covenant, a party of them throws it aside? But most of them do not believe." And you see from this that their argument with the Prophet was like the argument of their forefathers with Moses, the argument of obstinate people who do not seek guidance nor desire correctness nor want a truth to support, but rather falsehood, twisting their tongues with it, yet despite that, the Prophet would treat them with gentleness, kindness, and deliberation at times, and firmness at other times.

The Prophet's argument with the Christians: As for his argument with the Christians, it was little, due to their distance from him and their lack of mixing with Muslims except rarely, and the Prophet in his argument with them would attack the doctrine of the Trinity and show their disbelief in it, as God said: "They have certainly disbelieved who say, 'Indeed...'"
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a historical and scriptural exposition of the dialectical method (jadal) employed by the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) in his engagements with the People of the Book, i.e., Jews and Christians. The author begins by grounding the practice of dialectic in Quranic injunctions, citing verses that command disputing with the People of the Book in a manner that is best (Quran 29:46 and 16:125). The author then recounts several instances of Jewish arguments against the Prophet: they claimed Abraham as their own, but the Quran refutes this by stating Abraham was a hanif (monotheist) not a Jew or Christian. They objected to abrogation (naskh) in Islamic law, and the Quran responded that abrogation is God's prerogative and He brings something better. They demanded a sign other than the Quran, claiming a covenant from God requiring a fire-consumed offering, and the Quran exposed their inconsistency by noting they had killed previous messengers despite such signs. They asked the Prophet to bring down a book from heaven, and the Quran compares this to the Israelites' demand to see God, which led to their destruction.

The author then narrates a detailed encounter from Ibn Hisham's Sira, where Jewish rabbis posed four questions to test the Prophet's prophethood. The Prophet responded by invoking their own scriptures and knowledge, using a method of swearing by God and asking them to affirm truths known to them. This illustrates the dialectical technique of appealing to common ground and accepted knowledge (ilm) to establish credibility. The questions cover embryology, the nature of prophetic sleep, the self-imposed prohibition of Jacob, and the identity of the archangel Gabriel. When the rabbis admitted the truth of his answers but rejected him due to enmity to Gabriel, the Quran was revealed condemning their enmity. The author concludes that their jadal was that of obstinate people (mut'annitin) who seek not truth but falsehood, yet the Prophet responded with a mix of gentleness and firmness.

Finally, the author notes that the Prophet's jadal with Christians was limited, focusing on refuting the doctrine of the Trinity, citing the Quranic verse that declares disbelief in those who say God is one of three. The passage thus serves both as a historical account and a model for proper dialectical engagement: relying on revelation, appealing to common ground, and balancing softness with resoluteness.

مُتَعَنِّت
جَدَل

Key Takeaway
Obstinate disputation (muta'annit) is characterized by rejecting truth after it is recognized, and the Prophetic response balances gentleness with resoluteness.

Unity of Quranic and Prophetic Jadal
Author asserts the inseparable unity between Quranic and Prophetic jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the inseparable relationship between Quranic and Prophetic jadal and identify two logical fallacies (dawr and tasalsul) that the Prophet (PBUH) is reported to have refuted.

Core Idea
Prophetic jadal is a direct continuation of Quranic methodology, grounded in logical precision.

This passage opens a new section on the jadal found in the Prophetic Sunnah. The author makes a strong claim: there is a seamless unity between the jadal of the Qur'an and that of the Prophet. He then highlights that the Prophet did not just argue rhetorically—he employed precise logical tools like identifying legal causes, distinguishing between valid and invalid attributes, and cutting off circular reasoning (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul). This sets the stage for the concrete examples that will follow, demonstrating the legitimacy and importance of jadal in Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central thesis
Read the first two sentences. The author states that Quranic jadal and Prophetic jadal are a cohesive unity with no separation. This is the main claim of the passage.
2
Locate the Prophet's logical activities
Find where the passage lists what the Prophet explained: legal causes (al-‘ilal al-shar‘iyya), objections (ma’akhidh), differences (furūq), considered and disregarded attributes, and the fallacies dawr and tasalsul. The last two are key logical concepts.
Dawr = circular reasoning; tasalsul = infinite regress.
3
Understand the source of the Prophet's method
The passage attributes the Prophet's knowledge to divine revelation and his understanding of the Qur’an. This grounds Prophetic jadal in revelation, not merely human reasoning.
4
Recognize the author's purpose
The author announces he will present examples from the Sunnah to illustrate how the Prophet used concise arguments to establish truth, especially with his Companions. This increases the importance and legitimacy of jadal.

Source Text

المطلب الثالث : من مسالك الجدل في السنة النبوية بين الجدل القرآني وبين الجدل الذي ورد في السنة النبوية وحدة متماسكة لا انفصام لها .. وإن آثار الجدل القرآني قد ظهرت واضحة جلية في التوجيه والجدال الذي كان يقوم به رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في تبليغ رسالة الله عندما تدعو الحاجة إلى استخدام ذلك النوع من الجدل المحكم... وقد بين رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم العلل الشرعية والمآخذ والفروق والأوصاف المعتبرة والأوصاف الملغاة وبين الدور والتسلسل ،وقطعهما، وكل هذا مما آتاه الله من الوحي وفهمه من منهج القرآن المنزل عليه، وبهذا كانت العلاقة واضحة في تأثير الجدل القرآني في السنة المطهرة . وسنورد فيما يلي نماذج من الجدل الذي ورد في السنة، وكيف أوضح رسول الله له فيه الحق بأوجز عبارة وأخصر طريق، وسترى من هذه النماذج أن أكثرها مما دار بين النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ،وصحابته، وهو ما يزيد المنهج الجدلي أهمية، ويبرز مشروعيته، ومن ذلك ما يلي
Third demand: From the methods of jadal in the Prophetic Sunnah. Between Quranic jadal and the jadal that occurred in the Prophetic Sunnah is a cohesive unity with no separation. Indeed, the effects of Quranic jadal have appeared clearly and manifestly in the guidance and argumentation that the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, undertook in conveying the message of Allah when the need called for using that type of solid jadal. And the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, explained the legal causes, objections, differences, considered attributes, disregarded attributes, and explained circular reasoning and infinite regress and cut them off. All of this is from what Allah gave him of revelation and understanding from the methodology of the revealed Qur'an. And thus the relationship was clear in the influence of Quranic jadal upon the purified Sunnah. And we will present below examples of jadal that occurred in the Sunnah, and how the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, clarified the truth in them with the most concise expression and shortest path. And you will see from these examples that most of them occurred between the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and his Companions, and that increases the importance of the dialectical method and highlights its legitimacy. And from that are the following.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage introduces the relationship between Quranic jadal and Prophetic jadal. It opens by stating that there is an inseparable unity between the two. The author then explains that the Prophet's jadal was a direct application of the Quranic method, clarifying legal causes, objections, differences between attributes that are considered effective and those that are disregarded, and also explaining and cutting off circular reasoning (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul). The passage emphasizes that the Prophet's knowledge of these logical tools came from divine revelation and his deep understanding of the Qur'an. The author then announces that he will present examples from the Sunnah to illustrate how the Prophet, often with his Companions, used concise and powerful arguments to establish the truth, thereby demonstrating the importance and legitimacy of the jadal method. This section sets the stage for the specific examples that follow.

The Prophetic Refutation of Circularity and Infinite Regress
Refutation of dawr and tasalsul via a hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical fallacies of circularity (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul) in a causal argument, using the Prophet's question as a template.

Core Idea
One question exposes both logical impossibilities in one stroke.

This lesson examines a powerful example of logical reasoning from the Sunnah. The Prophet (PBUH) refutes the superstitious belief in independent contagion by posing a single question that forces the listener to confront two logical absurdities: circular reasoning and infinite regress. Ibn al-Qayyim unpacks the logical force of this question, showing how it dismantles the underlying causal claim.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
A Bedouin observes that a mangy camel causes other camels to become mangy. He implies that contagion works by itself.
2
Identify the Prophet's question
The Prophet asks: 'Who infected the first (mangy camel)?' This question targets the origin of the infection.
The first camel is the index case.
3
Recognize the two possibilities
The first camel's mange must come from something. If from another camel, that leads to an infinite chain; if from itself, that is circular.
Ibn al-Qayyim clarifies these two branches.
4
Apply the logical refutation
Both possibilities are impossible: infinite regress is rationally invalid, and circularity is also impossible. Therefore, contagion is not an independent cause.
This shows that causation ultimately rests with Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «لا عدوى ولا صفر» ولا هامة فقال أعرابي : ما بال الإبل تكون في الرمل، كأنها الظباء، فيخالطها البعير الأجرب فيجربها؟ قال: «فمن أعدى الأول؟!» قال ابن القيم : انظر ... كيف اشتملت هذه الكلمة الوجيزة المختصرة البينة على إبطال الدور والتسلسل فقال من أوتي جوامع الكلم: «فمن أعدى الأول؟!» ، ففهم السامع من هذا أن إعداء الأول كان من إعداء غيره له، فإنه إن لم ينته إلى غاية فهو التسلسل في المؤثرات، وهو باطل بصريح العقل، وإن انتهى إلى غاية وقد استفادت الجرب من إعداء من جرب له فهو الدور الممتنع
On the authority of Abu Huraira, may Allah be pleased with him, who said: The Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said: 'There is no contagion and no safar' and no hama. A Bedouin asked: 'What is the matter with camels that are in the sand, like gazelles, and a mangy camel intermixes with them and they become mangy?' He said: 'Then who infected the first?' Ibn al-Qayyim said: 'Look... how this concise, brief, clear word contained the nullification of circularity and infinite regress. For he, who was given the comprehensive speech, said: 'Then who infected the first?' The listener understood from this that the infection of the first came from the infection of someone else infecting him. If it does not end at a limit, then it is infinite regress in causes, which is invalid by clear reason. If it ends at a limit, and the mange benefited from the infection of someone infected with mange for it, then it is impossible circularity.'
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage presents an example from the Sunnah illustrating the refutation of circular reasoning (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul). The author narrates a hadith where the Prophet (PBUH) asserts that there is no contagion by itself, and a Bedouin raises an objection: how do camels catch mange from a mangy camel? The Prophet responds with a powerful rhetorical question: 'Who infected the first (mangy camel)?' The author then quotes Ibn al-Qayyim's commentary, explaining the logical force of this question. Ibn al-Qayyim points out that the question dismantles two possibilities: either the infection of the first camel came from another cause, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul) which is rationally invalid, or the first camel caused itself, which is circular (dawr) and also impossible. Thus, the only coherent conclusion is that contagion does not occur by itself but by the will of Allah. This example shows how a concise Prophetic argument effectively refutes logical fallacies.

Key Terms
دور
تسلسل
عدوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) That both dawr and tasalsul are invalid, so contagion is not an independent cause
C) That camels cannot catch mange from each other
D) That the Bedouin's question was invalid

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's question provides a concise logical refutation of both circularity and infinite regress in causal arguments.

Proving the Effective Cause through Dawran (Rotation)
Author introduces the method of proving the 'illa via dawran with a Prophetic example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the method of dawran and identify how it is used to establish the 'illa of a ruling.

Core Idea
Dawran identifies the 'illa by observing co-variation: when the cause is present, the ruling is present.

In Islamic legal theory, identifying the effective cause ('illa) of a ruling is crucial for analogical reasoning (qiyas). One powerful method is dawran (rotation), which relies on observing the concurrence of the cause and the ruling. This passage introduces the method with a Prophetic example, though the text is incomplete. We will explore the logical foundation of dawran and its application.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Concept of 'Illa
The effective cause is the attribute that necessitates a ruling. In dawran, we look for an attribute that coincides with the ruling in both presence and absence.
2
The Principle of Co-variation
If when the attribute is present the ruling is present, and when the attribute is absent the ruling is absent, then the attribute is the 'illa. This is the core of dawran.
Do not confuse dawran with circular reasoning (dawr); they are distinct concepts.
3
Example from the Sunnah (Ibn al-Lutbiyya)
The story: The Prophet appointed Ibn al-Lutbiyya as a zakat collector. When he returned, he brought gifts. The Prophet objected, indicating that gift-giving by officials is prohibited. Through dawran, the 'illa is identified as the act of giving gifts while holding public office, which co-varies with the ruling of prohibition.
The passage is incomplete; the full story from hadith sources provides the complete illustration.
4
Apply Dawran to a New Case
Given a ruling (e.g., prohibition of khamr) and a candidate attribute (intoxication), check if the attribute co-varies with the ruling. If intoxication is present, prohibition exists; if not, prohibition is absent. Thus, intoxication is the 'illa.
This method requires that the attribute and ruling be perfectly concurrent in all relevant instances.

Source Text

Proving the effective cause by the method of rotation: from that which is reported in the story of Ibn al-Lutbiyya. On the authority of Abu Humayd al-Sa'idi, may Allah be pleased with him, who said: The Messenger of Allah employed a man over the charity of Banu Sulaym called Ibn al-Lutbiyya. When...
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage introduces the second example from the Sunnah: proving the effective cause ('illa) through rotation (dawran). The author mentions the story of Ibn al-Lutbiyya, but the text cuts off before the full narrative is given. In the complete story, the Prophet (PBUH) objects to Ibn al-Lutbiyya's actions, and through the principle of rotation (that when the cause is present the ruling is present, and when absent the ruling is absent), the 'illa is identified. This method is a standard tool in Islamic legal theory (usul al-fiqh) for establishing causality. Due to the incomplete text, the full illustration of dawran is not provided here.

Effective Distinction in Dialectical Reasoning
Explains the Prophet's method of distinguishing rulings by effective differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the effective distinction (al-farq al-mu'aththir) between two cases in a legal debate and verify that it affects the ruling.

Core Idea
A ruling difference is only valid if based on an operative attribute, not a superficial one.

In dialectical reasoning, a key skill is distinguishing between cases that share a general rule but may be treated differently due to a specific attribute. This passage shows the Prophet's method: he compared camels and sheep, identified a real difference in their nature, and used that to justify different rulings. Learn to identify such effective distinctions in usul al-fiqh debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases being compared
Find the two entities whose rulings are being distinguished. In this passage, they are camels and sheep.
2
Extract the attribute mentioned for each
List the specific characteristics given: camels are self-sufficient and independent; sheep need a shepherd and are vulnerable to predators if unattended.
3
Check if the attribute is operative (mu'aththir)
Ask whether the attribute logically affects the ruling. Here, self-sufficiency reduces the need for guardianship, so it directly impacts the ruling on liability.
4
Confirm the distinction is not superficial
Ensure the distinction is not based on irrelevant factors like size or value. The Prophet only cited operative attributes.
5
Apply to other debates
Practice by considering two similar cases in fiqh and identifying an attribute that would justify different rulings. Test if it is truly operative.
This skill is central to jadal (dialectical argumentation) in usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ففرق النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بين الحكمين باستغناء الإبل واستقلالها بنفسها دون أن يخاف عليها الهلكة في البرية واحتياج الغنم إلى راع وحافظ وأنه إن غاب عنها فهي عرضة للسباع بخلاف الإبل، فهكذا تكون الفروق المؤثرة في الأحكام.
The Prophet distinguished between the two rulings by the self-sufficiency of camels and their independence without fear of perishing in the wilderness, and the need of sheep for a shepherd and guardian, and that if they are absent they are exposed to predators unlike camels. Thus are the effective distinctions in rulings.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage presents an example of an effective distinction (al-farq al-mu'aththir) in jurisprudential reasoning, a key tool in dialectical argumentation. The Prophet compared two cases: camels and sheep. Camels are self-sufficient and can survive in the wild without constant care, so leaving them unattended is not considered neglect. Sheep, however, require a shepherd and are vulnerable to predators if left alone. This difference in their natures justifies different rulings regarding responsibility for their care. The lesson is that a distinction between two cases must be based on a real, operative attribute that affects the ruling, not on arbitrary grounds.

Key Terms
الفروق المؤثرة

Key Takeaway
Effective distinctions must be based on attributes that inherently affect the ruling, not arbitrary differences.

Rejecting Qiyas that Contradicts Text
Uses a hadith to establish two rules for rejecting analogy that opposes a textual ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an analogical extension is invalid because it contradicts an explicit textual limitation, using the two rules from the hadith.

Core Idea
An explicit textual limitation blocks analogical extension beyond its scope.

In usul al-fiqh, an explicit text (nass) takes precedence over analogy (qiyas). This lesson uses a famous hadith to establish two rules that prevent unwarranted analogical extension: the ruling is tied to the specific object, and the rationale (illah) must match. You will learn to spot when a qiyas violates these rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the textual limitation
In the hadith, the Prophet says 'Only its eating is forbidden.' Locate the exact phrase that limits the prohibition. This is the nass that restricts the ruling.
The word 'innama' (only) is key—it indicates exclusivity.
2
Extract the first rule: specificity of ruling
The author says the text is not ambiguous (ghayr mujmal) and the prohibition is attached to the specific object (al-a'yan). This means the ruling applies only to what the text specifies, not to every possible use.
Rule 1: Rulings on objects are not automatically generalised to all attributes.
3
Extract the second rule: cutting off analogy
The author says the analogy linking skin use to meat eating is invalid (bāṭil). The rationale for forbidding meat (internal consumption) does not apply to skin after tanning (external use). Thus, qiyas is rejected because the 'illah is different.
Rule 2: Analogy is invalid if the 'illah of the original does not apply to the new case.
4
Apply the rules to a hypothetical objection
Imagine someone argues: 'Since eating is forbidden, using the skin should also be forbidden by qiyas.' Check: Does the text limit the prohibition? Yes, to eating. Is the 'illah (internal consumption) present in skin use? No, after tanning it is external. Therefore, the qiyas is invalid.
This demonstrates practical application of both rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رد القياس إذا عارض النص : عن ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما قال : تصدق على مولاة لميمونة بشاة فماتت، فمر بها رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال : «هلا أخذتم إهابها فدبغتموه فانتفعتم به» فقالوا : إنها ميتة، فقال: «إنما حرم أكلها» وتأمل قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم في الميتة : إنما حرم أكلها كيف تضمن التفرقة بين أكل اللحم واستعمال الجلد وبين أن النص إنما تناول تحريم الأكل، وهذا تحته قاعدتان عظيمتان: إحداهما : بيان أن التحليل والتحريم المضافين إلى الأعيان غير مجمل وأنه غير مراد به من كل عين ما هي مهيأة له والثانية : قطع إلحاق استعمال الجلد بأكل اللحم وأنه لا يصح قياسه عليه، فلو أن قائلا قال : وإن دلت على تحريم الأكل وحده فتحريم ملابسة الجلد قياسا عليه، كان قياسه باطلا بالنص، إذ لا يلزم من تحريم الملابسة الباطنية تحريم ملابسة الجلد ظاهرا بعد الدباغ، ففي هذا الحديث بيان المراد من الآية وبيان فساد إلحاق الجلد باللحم
Rejecting qiyas when it contradicts the text: From Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both, said: A sheep was given in charity to the freed slave of Maymunah, and it died. The Messenger of Allah passed by it and said: 'Why did you not take its skin, tan it, and benefit from it?' They said: It is carrion (maytah). He said: 'Only its eating is forbidden.' And reflect on his saying, peace be upon him, concerning carrion: 'Only its eating is forbidden' – how it implied a distinction between eating the meat and using the skin, and that the text only addresses the prohibition of eating. Under this are two great rules: First: that prohibition and permission attributed to specific objects are not ambiguous, and they are not intended to cover every possible use of that object. Second: cutting off the annexation of using the skin to eating the meat, and that it is not valid to analogize it to it. So if someone said: Even if it indicates the prohibition of eating alone, the prohibition of touching the skin by analogy with it, his analogy is invalid by the text, because the prohibition of internal contact does not necessitate the prohibition of external contact with the skin after tanning. In this hadith is clarification of the intended meaning of the verse and clarification of the invalidity of annexing the skin to the meat.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage explains the principle of rejecting qiyas (analogical reasoning) when it contradicts an explicit text (nass). It uses the famous hadith about the sheep that died: the Prophet permitted using the skin after tanning, despite the general prohibition of carrion, clarifying that the text only forbids eating. From this, the author extracts two great rules (qa'idatan 'azimatani). First, when something is declared forbidden or permitted, the ruling is specifically tied to the object itself, not to every possible attribute or use. Second, it is invalid to analogize the use of the skin to the eating of the meat, because the prohibition is limited to internal consumption (akl) and does not extend to external use (istimal) after treatment. The passage thus demonstrates how a proper understanding of the text prevents unwarranted analogical extension.

Key Terms
القياس
النص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because the Prophet explicitly prohibited all use of carrion.
C) Because the text limits the prohibition to eating, and the rationale (internal consumption) does not apply to external use after tanning.
D) Because sheep are different from other animals.

Key Takeaway
An explicit textual limitation (like 'only') prevents analogical extension to aspects not covered by the text, especially when the rationale differs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Preferring the Preventer over the Requirer
Analyses the Hadith of Hatib to illustrate the principle of tarjih al-mani' over al-muqtadi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the roles of al-mani' and al-muqtadi in a legal scenario and explain how the Hadith of Hatib supports the principle that a preventer requires an underlying requirer.

Core Idea
A preventer can block a ruling only when the requirer for that ruling is present.

In dialectical legal theory, rulings are often determined by competing factors: what requires a ruling (muqtadi) and what prevents it (mani'). The Hadith of Hatib provides a clear case: spying required killing, but attending Badr prevented it. Understanding how the Prophet weighed these factors illustrates a foundational principle in usul al-fiqh and munazara.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Requirer (al-muqtadi)
What factor in this story calls for a specific legal ruling? The spying of Hatib required his execution according to the general rule for treason.
The muqtadi is the cause for the ruling.
2
Identify the Preventer (al-mani')
What factor blocks the application of the ruling despite the presence of the cause? Hatib's participation in Badr prevented his execution because of divine forgiveness.
The man' is a countervailing factor.
3
Analyze the Dialectical Relationship
Why does this hadith support the principle that a preventer can operate only when a requirer exists? The Prophet did not simply say Hatib is forgiven; he acknowledged the requirer (spying) and overrode it with the preventer (Badr). This shows that the man' presupposes the muqtadi.
This is the key insight for the disputed usul principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
5 - ترجيح المانع على المقتضي : من ذلك؛ أن عمر استأذن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في قتل حاطب بن أبي بلتعة لما راسل المشركين يخبرهم بمقدم رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عليهم، فقال عليه الصلاة والسلام : «إنه شهد بدرًا وما يدريك لعل الله أن يكون قد اطلع على أهل بدر، فقال اعملوا ما شئتم فقد غفرت لكم» . فهذا الحديث لرسول الله لا يتضمن حكم القاعدة التي اختلف فيها أرباب الجدل والأصوليون وهي أن التعليل بالمانع هل يفتقر إلى قيام المقتضي؟ فعلل النبي هو عصمة دمه بشهوده بدرًا دون الإسلام العام، فدل على أن مقتضى قتله كان قد وجد وعارض سبب العصمة وهو التجسس على رسول الله ، ولكن عارض هذا المقتضي مانع منع من تأثيره وهو شهوده بدرا، وقد سبق من الله مغفرته لمن شهدها وعلى هذا فالحديث حجة لمن رأى قتل الجاسوس لأنه ليس ممن شهد بدرًا وإنما امتنع قتل حاطب لشهوده بدرًا.
5 - Preferring the Preventer over the Requirer: From that is that 'Umar asked the Messenger of Allah for permission to kill Hatib ibn Abi Balta'ah when he corresponded with the polytheists informing them of the Messenger's approach. He said, peace be upon him: 'He attended Badr, and what do you know? Perhaps Allah has looked upon the people of Badr and said: Do whatever you wish, for I have forgiven you.' This hadith of the Messenger does not contain the ruling of the principle about which the masters of dialectic and usulis disagreed, namely: Does causation by a preventer require the existence of a requirer? The Prophet caused the inviolability of his blood by his attendance at Badr, not by general Islam. This indicates that the requirement for his killing had existed and opposed the cause of inviolability, which was spying on the Messenger, but this requirer was opposed by a preventer that prevented its effect, namely his attendance at Badr. And Allah had previously granted forgiveness to those who attended it. On this basis, the hadith is evidence for those who hold that a spy should be killed if he did not attend Badr, and that the killing of Hatib was prevented only because of his attendance at Badr.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage discusses the principle of 'preferring the preventer over the requirer' (tarjih al-mani' 'ala al-muqtadi). It uses the story of Hatib ibn Abi Balta'ah, who spied for the polytheists. 'Umar wanted to kill him, but the Prophet prevented it, citing Hatib's participation in the Battle of Badr. The author explains that this hadith addresses a disputed point in usul: whether a preventing factor (man') can only be considered if there is an existing requirer (muqtadi) for the ruling. Here, the requirer for killing (spying) existed, but it was overridden by the preventer (attendance at Badr). Thus, the hadith supports the view that a preventer can block the effect of a requirer even if the requirer is present. This illustrates the dialectical principle of weighing competing factors in legal reasoning.

Key Terms
المانع
المقتضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hadith of Hatib demonstrates that a preventer (mani') can override a requirer (muqtadi) only when the requirer is actually present, illustrating the principle of tarjih al-mani' 'ala al-muqtadi.

Analogical Reasoning in the Prophetic Hadith
Uses the hadith about kissing while fasting to illustrate analogical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the 'illa (effective cause) in an analogical argument and assess whether two cases are truly analogous.

Core Idea
Analogy in fiqh depends on a shared 'illa—not superficial similarity.

This passage from the hadith teaches us how the Prophet used analogical reasoning to clarify a ruling. 'Umar thought kissing invalidates the fast, but the Prophet compared it to rinsing, which is permissible. We will dissect the structure of this analogy to see how it works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original case (asl)
Find the case that already has a known ruling. Here, rinsing the mouth with water while fasting is the asl.
The Prophet asks about rinsing, which 'Umar knows is permissible.
2
Identify the ruling (hukm) of the asl
Determine what the ruling is for the original case. For rinsing, it is permissible and does not break the fast.
'Umar states 'there is no harm in that'.
3
Identify the new case (far')
The case whose ruling is being sought. Here, kissing while fasting is the far'.
'Umar expresses concern about kissing.
4
Identify the 'illa (effective cause)
The shared attribute that justifies extending the ruling. The 'illa is that neither rinsing nor kissing involves anything reaching the stomach.
The Prophet implicitly relies on this common attribute.
5
Confirm the analogy is valid
Check that the 'illa applies to both cases. Since both are actions in the mouth without swallowing, the ruling of permissibility is extended.
The Prophet concludes 'then it is likewise'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
6 - تشبيه الشيء بنظيره وإلحاقه به : عن جابر بن عبد الله رضي الله عنه قال : قال عمر بن الخطاب رضي الله عنه : هششت وقبلت وأنا صائم، فقلت: يا رسول الله صنعت اليوم أمرًا عظيمًا، قبلت وأنا صائم ، قال : أرأيت لو تمضمضت من الماء وأنت صائم؟»، قلت : لا بأس به، قال : «فمه»
6 - Likening a thing to its similar and annexing it to it: From Jabir ibn 'Abdullah, may Allah be pleased with him, said: 'Umar ibn al-Khattab said: I became excited and kissed while I was fasting. I said: O Messenger of Allah, I have done a great matter today—I kissed while fasting. He said: 'Do you think if you rinsed your mouth with water while fasting?' I said: There is no harm in that. He said: 'Then it is likewise.'
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents an example of analogical reasoning (tashbih al-shay' bi-nazirihi) used by the Prophet. 'Umar was concerned that kissing might break his fast. The Prophet responded by drawing an analogy: rinsing the mouth with water does not break the fast, so kissing, which does not involve ingestion, is similarly permissible. The underlying principle is that the effective attribute ('illa) is the absence of anything reaching the stomach. Since both rinsing and kissing only involve the mouth and do not involve swallowing, they are analogous. This illustrates how valid analogy works in Islamic law: the new case (kissing) is annexed to the original case (rinsing) because they share the same operative attribute.

Key Terms
تشبيه
نظير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Kissing is like rinsing the mouth with water.
C) Kissing is like drinking.
D) Kissing is like sexual intercourse.

Key Takeaway
Valid analogy in Islamic law requires a shared 'illa between the original and new case.

Dialectical Method of the Prophet in Answering 'Umar
Uses the hadith about kissing while fasting to illustrate analogical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dialectical moves in a Prophetic response to a legal query.

Core Idea
The Prophet responded not by direct prohibition but by drawing an analogy, which is a dialectical move to guide the questioner.

In this exchange, 'Umar presents his action as a problem. Instead of issuing a simple ruling, the Prophet uses a questioning method (istifham) to lead 'Umar to the correct conclusion. This is a model of dialectical teaching.

Guided Path
1
Identify 'Umar's position
'Umar states he kissed while fasting and considers it a 'great matter,' implying he believes it might break the fast.
He seeks confirmation of his concern.
2
Identify the Prophet's counter-question
The Prophet asks: 'Do you think if you rinsed your mouth with water while fasting?' This is a dialectical move that shifts the focus.
The question is rhetorical, designed to invoke analogical thinking.
3
Analyze 'Umar's response
'Umar admits that rinsing is no problem. By doing so, he implicitly accepts the premise that actions not involving swallowing are permissible.
This admission becomes the foundation for the analogy.
4
Understand the Prophet's conclusion
The Prophet then says 'Then it is likewise,' applying the same ruling to kissing. This is the dialectical resolution: 'Umar is led to the conclusion by his own reasoning.
The Prophet avoids direct command; he lets the analogy speak.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
6 - تشبيه الشيء بنظيره وإلحاقه به : عن جابر بن عبد الله رضي الله عنه قال : قال عمر بن الخطاب رضي الله عنه : هششت وقبلت وأنا صائم، فقلت: يا رسول الله صنعت اليوم أمرًا عظيمًا، قبلت وأنا صائم ، قال : أرأيت لو تمضمضت من الماء وأنت صائم؟»، قلت : لا بأس به، قال : «فمه»
6 - Likening a thing to its similar and annexing it to it: From Jabir ibn 'Abdullah, may Allah be pleased with him, said: 'Umar ibn al-Khattab said: I became excited and kissed while I was fasting. I said: O Messenger of Allah, I have done a great matter today—I kissed while fasting. He said: 'Do you think if you rinsed your mouth with water while fasting?' I said: There is no harm in that. He said: 'Then it is likewise.'
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents an example of analogical reasoning (tashbih al-shay' bi-nazirihi) used by the Prophet. 'Umar was concerned that kissing might break his fast. The Prophet responded by drawing an analogy: rinsing the mouth with water does not break the fast, so kissing, which does not involve ingestion, is similarly permissible. The underlying principle is that the effective attribute ('illa) is the absence of anything reaching the stomach. Since both rinsing and kissing only involve the mouth and do not involve swallowing, they are analogous. This illustrates how valid analogy works in Islamic law: the new case (kissing) is annexed to the original case (rinsing) because they share the same operative attribute.

Key Terms
تشبيه
نظير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What dialectical technique does the Prophet use in responding to 'Umar?
A) Direct prohibition
B) Making an analogy through a rhetorical question
C) Quoting a verse from the Quran
D) Changing the subject

Key Takeaway
The Prophetic response model uses analogy through questioning, which is an effective dialectical tool for teaching.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the hadith, identify the crucial question the Prophet uses to trigger the analogy. Then, rewrite this exchange as a formal jadal dialogue, labelling the positions and responses.
?
Think of another fiqh issue where a similar dialectical method could be used (e.g., is using perfume while fasting allowed?). Write a short dialogue using the same structure: statement, counter-question, admission, conclusion.

The First Jadal: Succession at Saqifa Bani Sa'ida
Describes the first political jadal among Companions regarding succession and caliph conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the three positions debated at Saqifa Bani Sa'ida and identify the structure of the jadal exchange.

Core Idea
Jadal among the Companions began with the first succession crisis.

The passage shows that jadal emerged immediately after the Prophet's death as a practical tool for resolving political leadership. The Saqifa event provides a concrete historical example of dialectical reasoning among the Companions. By analyzing it, we see how jadal was used to argue for different candidates based on merits and qualifications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputants
Find the three groups mentioned in the passage: the Ansar, the Muhajirun led by Abu Bakr, and the Hashimite faction calling for Ali.
Each group represents a distinct position on who should be caliph.
2
Extract each group's main argument
The Ansar argued from their role as helpers and defenders of Islam. The Muhajirun claimed leadership due to precedence and closeness to the Prophet. The Hashimites pointed to Ali's personal merits.
These arguments are based on different criteria: tribal merit, religious precedence, and personal virtue.
3
Identify the resolution
The passage states that the middle group (Muhajirun) prevailed and the masses followed them, ending the dispute.
The resolution was not based on formal jadal rules but on consensus and political reality.
4
Classify the dialectical moves
Each group presented claims (da'wa) and presumably responded to objections. Though not detailed, this is a foundational example of jadal in action.
Later jadal theory formalizes such exchanges with rules for mu'arada (objection) and jawab (response).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أ - الجدل في الخلافة :
وكانت هذه أول مسألة تثير الجدل فيما بينهم، بل لقد كادت تفضي بهم إلى الخصام والنزاع، فقد شهدت سقيفة بني ساعدة أول حوار جدلي بين المسلمين بعد وفاة النبي ، وكان موضوع الحوار يدور حول من يخلف النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام، وما هي الشروط التي ينبغي أن تتوفر في هذا الخليفة حتى يشغل هذا المنصب .

A - Jadal in the Caliphate: This was the first issue that sparked jadal among them; indeed, it almost led them to quarrels and conflict. The Saqifa of Bani Sa'ida witnessed the first dialectical dialogue among Muslims after the Prophet's death. The topic of dialogue revolved around who would succeed the Prophet (peace be upon him) and what conditions should be met in this caliph to occupy this position. The Ansar held that the caliph should be from them, due to their virtue of giving refuge and support, and because they were the defenders of Islam, the supporters of the Messenger (PBUH), and the callers to it. They did not think that the Prophet designated his caliphate to a specific lineage of Arabs nor to a specific tribe. Another group, led by Abu Bakr, held that the matter belonged to the Muhajirun. A third group placed it in the Hashimite clan and called for Ali due to his distinction among the Hashimites in precedence in Islam, defense in momentous situations, knowledge, and understanding of religion. The disagreement did not last long, for the middle group overcame the other two and the masses of Muslims followed them. (1) Tarikh al-Jadal by Sheikh Muhammad Abu Zahra, p. 87. This is it. The jadal over the caliphate did not end at this limit. Besides the jadal about who was more entitled to the caliphate, another type of jadal later emerged revolving around how to conduct the affairs of the caliphate, the extent of the possibility of deposing the caliph if he lacked some conditions, and what the conditions were. That was the conflict over the caliphate during the era of Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) and later during the era of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him). It is not important for us to delve into this topic because it does not relate to our research here.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage describes the historical emergence of jadal (dialectical reasoning) among the Companions immediately after the Prophet's death. It identifies the first issue that sparked jadal as the question of succession (caliphate). The author narrates the famous event at Saqifa Bani Sa'ida, where the first dialectical dialogue took place. The positions of the Ansar, the Muhajirun led by Abu Bakr, and the Hashimite faction supporting Ali are outlined. The author notes that the disagreement was resolved quickly, with the middle group (the Muhajirun) prevailing. Then the author mentions a second phase of jadal concerning the governance and conditions of the caliph, occurring during the reigns of Uthman and Ali. However, the author states that this topic is not relevant to the current research, thus limiting the discussion to the first phase. The purpose of this passage is to provide historical context for the use of jadal in early Islamic political thought, showing that it was present from the very beginning.

The Birth of Jadal in Legal Rulings
Introduces jadal in furu' fiqhiyya after the Prophet's death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Companions turned to ijtihad by opinion and how this led to jadal in legal rulings.

Core Idea
When revelation ended, ijtihad by opinion began — and so did legitimate differences.

The Prophet’s death marked a pivotal shift: no new revelation. This lesson traces how the Companions naturally turned to ijtihad by opinion to address new problems, and why this inevitably produced differences of opinion — the very substrate of jadal in furu‘.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the end of revelation
Note that the Prophet's death stopped direct divine guidance. The Companions could no longer receive fresh texts for every new problem.
2
Identify the new reality
Companions faced unprecedented issues with no explicit Qur’anic or hadith text. They had to rely on the general principles they had learned.
3
Connect to ijtihad by opinion
This necessity led them to ijtihad bi al-ra’y — using reasoned judgment based on the spirit of the Shari‘a and its general rules.
This is distinct from ijtihad based on clear texts.
4
Understand why differences arose
Since ijtihad involves human reasoning, different Companions could reach different conclusions, leading to divergence in fatwas. This is the basis of jadal in furu‘.
The author stresses this was natural.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - الجدل في الفروع : إلى جانب الجدل في الأمور السياسية الذي رأينا استفحاله بين الصحابة، ظهر بينهم جدل في ميدان آخر ، هو ميدان الفروع الفقهية وهذا الميدان هو الذي يهمنا أساسا في هذا البحث. ذلك أن وفاة النبي و قد كانت إيذانا بانتهاء مرجعية الوحي التي كان الصحابة يئوبون إليها إذا ما واجهتهم مشكلة من المشكلات وأصبحوا أمام واقع جديد يطرح مشكلات جديدة لم يعرفوها من قبل ولا يعرفون في حكمها نصا من القرآن أو السنة، وقد كان طبيعيا أن يتجهوا إلى الاجتهاد بالرأي في تلك المسائل النصوص والقواعد العامة للشريعة التي تعلموها من طول صحبتهم
مستهدين بروح للنبي عليه الصلاة والسلام.
