وما دام الأمر متعلقا بالاجتهاد ،بالرأي، فقد كان طبيعيًا جدًّا أن يقع بينهم في نتائج هذا الاجتهاد، وهذا الاختلاف - عند الاستقراء – إنما يرجع إلى الأسباب الواقعية والموضوعية، من أهمها :
عدد من
1
لقد كان لتفرق الصحابة في الأمصار وما واجههم من أمواج الاستفسارات عن المسائل الفقهية وأحكامها تحت وطأة العادات والأعراف الجديدة وأنماط الحياة التي لا عهد لهم بها من قبل ؛ أثره الكبير في أن تختلف فتاوى الصحابة حسب اختلاف البلاد التي يقيمون فيها من حيث مدى القرب أو البعد عن طبيعة الجزيرة العربية ومهبط الوحي والرسالة، ففتاوى الصحابة الذين يقيمون بالشام والحجاز كانت أقرب إلى الانضباط الحرفي بالنصوص الثابتة في الكتاب أو السنة، وأبعد ما تكون - جهد الاستطاعة عن الرأي والاجتهاد المرسل، إذ
B - Jadal in Legal Rulings: Alongside the jadal in political affairs that we saw becoming rampant among the Companions, jadal appeared among them in another field: the field of legal rulings (furu' fiqhiyya). This field is what primarily concerns us in this research. That is because the death of the Prophet (PBUH) signaled the end of the reference of revelation to which the Companions would resort when facing a problem. They faced a new reality that posed new problems they had not known before and for which they knew no text from the Qur'an or Sunnah regarding their ruling. It was natural that they turned to ijtihad by opinion in those matters, guided by the spirit of the texts and the general rules of Shari'a that they had learned from their long companionship with the Prophet (PBUH). As long as the matter pertained to ijtihad by opinion, it was very natural that disagreement occurred among them in the results of this ijtihad. This disagreement - upon induction - is attributed to real and objective reasons, the most important of which are: a number of [reasons]. Foremost among them was the dispersal of the Companions in the various regions and the waves of inquiries they faced regarding legal issues and their rulings under the pressure of new customs, traditions, and lifestyles they had no previous experience with. This had a great effect in making the fatwas of the Companions differ according to the differences of the lands they resided in, in terms of the degree of proximity or distance from the nature of the Arabian Peninsula and the place of revelation and the message. So the fatwas of the Companions residing in Syria and Hijaz were closer to literal adherence to the established texts in the Book or Sunnah, and as far as possible from opinion and unrestricted ijtihad, when...
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage introduces the second major arena of jadal among the Companions: legal rulings (furu' fiqhiyya). The author notes that this field is the primary focus of the research. With the death of the Prophet, revelation ceased, and Companions faced new problems without direct textual answers. They resorted to ijtihad by opinion based on the spirit of Shari'a. The author argues that differences in ijtihad outcomes were natural and due to objective reasons. He then lists the first reason: the dispersion of Companions across different regions, leading to exposure to different customs and inquiries. This caused fatwas to vary; those in Syria and Hijaz tended to stick more literally to texts than those in other regions. The passage ends abruptly, but it clearly establishes the causal link between geographical dispersion and variation in fatwas, illustrating the role of jadal in legal reasoning.

C) The cessation of revelation and new problems
D) The orders of the caliphs

Key Takeaway
Ijtihad by opinion was a necessary response to new legal challenges after revelation ceased, and this naturally led to differences that fueled jadal in furu‘.

Geography’s Role in Ikhtilaf
Explains geographic dispersion as a reason for differing fatwas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify geographic dispersion as a key objective cause of differences in Companions' fatwas.

Core Idea
Where a Companion lived shaped how he made fatwas.

The author singles out the dispersal of Companions across regions as a major factor behind the variation in their legal opinions. This lesson examines how local customs and inquiries influenced fatwas, making jadal a reflection of geographic and social conditions.

Guided Path
1
Note the objective reasons
The author lists several objective reasons for ikhtilaf. First is the dispersal of Companions in different regions. Understand that this is a concrete, external cause, not personal whim.
2
Analyze the geographic effect
Companions in Syria and Hijaz were closer to the Arabian Peninsula and the original revelation; they adhered more literally to texts. Others farther away faced different pressures.
This is an example of how context shapes interpretation.
3
Connect to jadal
When Companions from different regions encountered each other or their fatwas conflicted, jadal emerged as they defended or challenged positions based on their regional contexts.
4
Evaluate the significance
Recognizing that differences were grounded in real circumstances helps us appreciate jadal as a tool for reconciling diversity, not a sign of disunity.

Types of Error in Companions' Ijtihad
Author lists types of error (jahl) in ijtihad with examples from the Companions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and categorize five types of error in Companions' ijtihad using the examples provided in the passage.

Core Idea
The Companions could err in ijtihad in multiple ways; jadal requires recognizing these errors.

In this passage from Jadal Usuliyyin, the author classifies types of ignorance or error that can occur in ijtihad among the Companions. By examining these categories and their examples, you will learn how to analyze historical ijtihad cases in jadal discussions. The examples illustrate that even the most knowledgeable Companions could be unaware of a ruling, doubt a report, misinterpret an action, or reconcile texts in a way that later proved incorrect.

Guided Path
1
Locate the first category: ignorance of a ruling
The author begins by mentioning that some Companions were unaware of rulings known to others. Identify the specific Companions and ruling mentioned (e.g., Muhammad ibn Maslamah, Abu Bakr, Umar, and the jizya from the Magians known to Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf).
This shows that knowledge is not equally distributed even among the most virtuous generation.
2
Identify the second category: doubt about a report
Find where the author discusses a Companion doubting the accuracy of a report. Note the example of Umar's action regarding the report of Fatima bint Qays about her divorce.
Here 'wahm' means a mistaken notion or doubt, not the logical faculty.
3
Recognize the third category: misinterpretation of Prophetic actions
The author gives the example of raml (quick walking) in tawaf. Explain why Ibn Abbas considered it a specific response to the polytheists' comment and not a sunnah, while others considered it a sunnah.
This illustrates how context can determine whether an action is sunnah or situational.
4
Analyze the fourth category: ijtihad followed by a contradicting text
The author provides the example of Abdullah ibn Amr ordering women to undo their braids during ghusl, and Aisha's correction based on her own practice with the Prophet. What does this illustrate about the fallibility of ijtihad?
This shows that even a Companion's ijtihad can be overturned by a better-informed text or practice.
5
Examine the fifth category: reconciling conflicting texts
The author discusses the mushāraka in inheritance, where Umar ruled that full siblings share with uterine siblings. Explain how Umar reconciled the Quranic verse (about kalala) with the hadith 'Give the fixed shares to their recipients...'
This demonstrates ijtihad in harmonizing primary sources, which can lead to different conclusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومحمد بن مسلمة وجهله أبو بكر وعمر، وكان حكم أخذ الجزية من المجوس عند عبد الرحمن بن عوف وجهله جمهور الصحابة. وربما يبلغه الحكم، أو الحديث، ولكنه يقع في نفسه أن راوي الخبر قد وهم، كفعل عمر في خبر فاطمة بنت قيس. أو أن يرى الصحابة الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم فعل فعلا فيحمله بعضهم على القربة، ويحمله بعضهم على أنه كان على وجه الاتفاق أو لسبب زال فلا يكون مطلوبًا لأمته؛ كالرمل في الطواف فذهب ابن عباس إلى أن الرسول فعل للسبب وهو قول المشركين عن المسلمين: أوهنتهم حمى يثرب، فذهب بذهاب سببه وليس بسنة، وقال غيره: إنه سنة. أو أن يجتهد أحدهم حين لا يجد نصّا، ثم يظهر النص بخلاف ما رأى؛ من ذلك ما أخرجه مسلم عن عبيد بن عمير قال: بلغ عائشة أن عبد الله بن عمرو يأمر النساء إذا اغتسلن أن ينقضن رؤوسهن فقالت عجبًا لابن عمرو هذا يأمر النساء إذا اغتسلن أن ينقضن رؤوسهن أفلا يأمرهن أن يحلقن رؤوسهن؟! لقد كنت أغتسل أنا ورسول الله له من إناء واحد ولا أزيد على أن أفرغ على رأسي ثلاث إفراغات. وربما اجتهد بعضهم في التوفيق بين القرآن والسنة لمعنى معتبر كالمشركة في الميراث، فقد جاء في القرآن قول الله عز وجل: وَإِن كَانَ رَجُلٌ يُورَثُ كَلَلَةٌ أَوِ امْرَأَةٌ وَلَهُ أَخٌ أَوْ أُخْتُ فَلِكُلِّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا السُّدُسُ فَإِن كَانُوا أَكْثَرَ مِن ذَلِكَ فَهُمْ شُرَكَاءُ فِي الثُّلثِ، ولا خلاف بين العلماء أن هذه الآية في ولد الأم، وجاء في الحديث المتفق عليه عن ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما أن النبي قال: ألحقوا الفرائض بأهلها فما بقي فلأولى رجل ذكر، فلما رأى عمر المشركة أن الإخوة الأشقاء شركاء لولد الأم، إذ أن أدنى أحوال الأقوى أن
And Muhammad ibn Maslamah, and Abu Bakr and Umar were ignorant of it. And the ruling on taking jizya from the Magians was [known] to Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf while the majority of the Companions were ignorant of it. And perhaps a Companion receives a ruling or hadith, but it occurs to him that the transmitter of the report may have been mistaken, like the action of Umar regarding the report of Fatima bint Qays. Or that a Companion sees the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, do an action, and some interpret it as an act of worship, while others interpret it as something done due to a specific circumstance or a reason that no longer exists, so it is not required for his community; like the raml in tawaf. Ibn Abbas held that the Messenger did it for a reason, which was the polytheists' saying about the Muslims: 'The fever of Yathrib has weakened them,' so it ceased with the cessation of its cause and is not a sunnah, while others said it is a sunnah. Or that one of them exercises ijtihad when he does not find a text, then a text appears contrary to his opinion; from that is what Muslim narrated from Ubayd ibn Umayr who said: It reached Aisha that Abdullah ibn Amr ordered women when they perform ghusl to undo their braids, so she said: 'How astonishing of Ibn Amr! He orders women when they perform ghusl to undo their braids; why does he not order them to shave their heads?! Indeed, I used to perform ghusl with the Messenger of God from a single vessel and I would do no more than pour three handfuls over my head.' And perhaps some of them exercise ijtihad in reconciling the Quran and the Sunnah based on a considered meaning, like the mushāraka in inheritance. For in the Quran came the word of God Almighty: 'And if a man is inherited as a kalāla, or a woman, and he has a brother or a sister, then each one of them has a sixth; and if they are more than that, then they are partners in the third.' And there is no disagreement among scholars that this verse is about the children of the mother. And came the agreed-upon hadith from Ibn Abbas that the Prophet said: 'Give the fixed shares to their recipients, and what remains goes to the nearest male relative.' So when Umar saw the mushāraka, that the full siblings are partners with the children of the mother, since the weakest state of the stronger is that...
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage from Jadal Usuliyyin presents several types of ignorance (jahl) or error that can occur in ijtihad among the Companions. The author uses historical examples to illustrate each type. First, he mentions that some Companions, like Muhammad ibn Maslamah, were unaware of certain rulings known to others, such as Abu Bakr and Umar. This shows that knowledge is not equally distributed. He then gives the example of Umar's initial verdict regarding the jizya from the Magians, where Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf had the correct knowledge while the majority were ignorant. Next, he discusses a scenario where a Companion receives a report but doubts its authenticity, like Umar's treatment of Fatima bint Qays's report about her divorce. This illustrates that even a Companion might question a report in his own mind. The third type involves seeing the Prophet perform an action and differing on whether it is a sunnah or a specific circumstance. The raml (quick walking) in tawaf is the example: Ibn Abbas considered it a specific response to the polytheists' comment, so it is not a permanent sunnah, while others considered it a sunnah. The fourth type occurs when a Companion exercises ijtihad in the absence of a text, but later a text appears contradicting his opinion. The example is Abdullah ibn Amr ordering women to undo their braids during ghusl, which Aisha corrected with her own practice. Finally, the author mentions ijtihad in reconciling conflicting texts, like the problem of 'mushāraka' in inheritance where Umar ruled that full siblings share with uterine siblings based on a meaning inferred from the Quran and hadith. The passage demonstrates that the Companions were not infallible in their reasoning and that jadal involves understanding these types of errors.

وهم
سنة
نص

The Companions could err in ijtihad in five distinct ways, which this passage classifies with historical examples.

?

Aisha's Reductio and Counterexample: Correcting Ijtihad with Text
Author illustrates a case where a sahaba's ijtihad contradicted a revealed text.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the use of reductio ad absurdum and adducing a counterexample from prophetic practice in a Companions' dialectic, and apply these tools to critique a flawed ijtihad.

Core Idea
Aisha corrected Ibn Amr by taking his ruling to its absurd conclusion and citing her own practice with the Prophet.

This passage illustrates how the Companions engaged in dialectic (jadal) to refine rulings when a text contradicted ijtihad. Aisha uses two tools: reductio ad absurdum (showing that Ibn Amr's logic implies an even more extreme ruling) and direct testimony of prophetic practice. You will learn to recognise these tools and see how they function within Islamic legal debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and its context
Ibn Amr ruled that women must undo their braids for ghusl. This was his ijtihad in the absence of a text.
2
Find the critical move: reductio ad absurdum
Aisha does not reject his ruling outright; she first reduces it to absurdity: if they must undo braids, why not shave heads? This highlights the logical flaw.
This is a form of qiyas al-aks (inverse analogy) or reductio.
3
Adduce the counterevidence
She then provides her own practice with the Prophet as a counterexample: they bathed from one vessel and she merely poured three handfuls over her head without undoing braids.
This is direct textual evidence from the sunna.
4
Understand the principle
When a text appears, ijtihad must yield. Here, the prophetic practice overrides the reasoned opinion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو أن يجتهد أحدهم حين لا يجد نصّا، ثم يظهر النص بخلاف ما رأى؛ من ذلك ما أخرجه مسلم عن عبيد بن عمير قال : بلغ عائشة أن عبد الله بن عمرو يأمر النساء إذا اغتسلن أن ينقضن رؤوسهن فقالت عجبًا لابن عمرو هذا يأمر النساء إذا اغتسلن أن ينقضن رؤوسهن أفلا يأمرهن أن يحلقن رؤوسهن؟! لقد كنت أغتسل أنا ورسول الله له من إناء واحد ولا أزيد على أن أفرغ على رأسي ثلاث إفراغات (2) .
Or that one of them makes ijtihad when he does not find a text, then the text appears contrary to what he opined; among that is what Muslim narrated from 'Ubayd ibn 'Umayr who said: It reached 'A'isha that 'Abdullah ibn 'Amr was commanding women when they bathe to undo their braids. She said: How strange of Ibn 'Amr! He commands women when they bathe to undo their braids; why doesn't he command them to shave their heads?! I used to bathe with the Messenger of Allah  from a single vessel and I did no more than pour three handfuls on my head (2).
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage presents a type of difference among the Companions (sahaba): when a scholar makes ijtihad in the absence of a text, then later a text appears that contradicts his view. The example given is that of 'Abdullah ibn 'Amr, who held that women must undo their braids when performing ghusl (ritual bath). This reached 'A'isha, who criticized his opinion by analogy: if they must undo their braids, then they might as well shave their heads! She provided evidence by reporting her own practice with the Prophet : they would bathe from one vessel and she would merely pour three handfuls over her head, without undoing her braids. This illustrates that the Companions valued adherence to revealed texts and were quick to correct opinions when a text became known. The passage is not a formal argument but a narrative example used to explain the dynamics of ijtihad and jadal among the early Muslims.

نص

Key Takeaway
Aisha showed that when a text appears, ijtihad must be corrected, and that reductio ad absurdum and adducing prophetic practice are powerful tools for jadal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Reconciling Quran and Sunnah: The Musharaka Case
Author discusses how Umar reconciled Quranic and hadith evidence in the inheritance case of al-musharaka.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyse how Umar reconciled Quranic and hadith evidence in a disputed inheritance case and identify the logical structure of his ruling.

Core Idea
When texts conflict, the stronger must share with the weaker to achieve justice.

This passage shows how Umar used ijtihad to reconcile two primary texts that seemed to give different inheritance shares. The case of al-musharaka involves full siblings and half-siblings from the mother. Understanding Umar's reasoning helps us see how jurists harmonise apparent contradictions without abandoning either text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent conflict
The Quran (4:12) gives half-siblings from the mother a share of one-sixth each or one-third total. The hadith 'Give the prescribed shares... what remains is for the nearest male relative' implies full siblings get the remainder if there are no other heirs. But when both types exist, the texts seem to conflict.
The conflict is only apparent; Umar sought to apply both texts justly.
2
Understand the positions
Full siblings are considered 'stronger' due to sharing both parents, while half-siblings from the mother are 'weaker'. The Quran gives the weaker a share; the hadith gives the stronger the remainder.
3
Examine Umar's reasoning
Umar argued that the 'lowest state of the stronger is that they be just and share with the weaker'. He concluded that full siblings, though stronger, should not take all the remainder; they should join the weaker in the one-third share, splitting it equally.
This reasoning prioritises justice and avoidance of injustice.
4
Note the objections and outcomes
Zayd and others objected with hypotheticals like 'suppose their father was a donkey', questioning the stronger claim of full siblings. Umar maintained his ruling, which became known as al-musharaka (or al-himariyya, etc.).
The names reflect the objections: 'donkey case', 'stone case'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما اجتهد بعضهم في التوفيق بين القرآن والسنة لمعنى معتبر كالمشركة في الميراث، فقد جاء في القرآن قول الله عز وجل : ... وَإِن كَانَ رَجُلٌ يُورَثُ كَلَلَةٌ أَوِ امْرَأَةٌ وَلَهُ أَخٌ أَوْ أُخْتُ فَلِكُلِ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا السُّدُسُ فَإِن كَانُوا أَكْثَرَ مِن ذَلِكَ فَهُمْ شُرَكَاءُ فِي الثُّلث ... (3) ، ولا خلاف بين العلماء أن هذه الآية في ولد الأم، وجاء في الحديث المتفق عليه عن ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما أن النبي قال : «ألحقوا الفرائض بأهلها فما بقي فلأولى رجل ذكر»(4)، فلما رأى عمر المشركة أن الإخوة الأشقاء شركاء لولد الأم ، إذ أن أدنى أحوال الأقوى أن هو العدل يشارك الأضعف، ولما قال له زيد : هب أن أباهم كان حمارًا، أو قال أحدهم: هب أن أبانا كان حجرًا في اليم، رأى عمر أن التشريك بينهم في الثلث يقسم بينهم بالسوية ذكورهم كإناثهم وسميت هذه الفريضة بالمشركة أو المشتركة أو الحمارية أو الحجرية أو اليمية (1) .
And sometimes some of them made ijtihad to reconcile between the Quran and the Sunnah for a valid meaning, like al-musharaka in inheritance. It came in the Quran, the saying of Allah Almighty: '... And if a man is inherited (kalala) or a woman, and he has a brother or a sister, then each of them has one-sixth; but if they are more than that, then they are partners in one-third...' (3). And there is no disagreement among the scholars that this verse is about siblings of the mother. And there came in the agreed-upon hadith from Ibn 'Abbas that the Prophet said: 'Give the prescribed shares to their rightful owners, and what remains is for the nearest male relative' (4). When 'Umar saw in al-musharaka that the full siblings share with the siblings of the mother, considering that the lowest state of the stronger (full siblings) is that they be just and share with the weaker (half-siblings from mother), and when Zayd said to him: 'Suppose their father was a donkey,' or someone said: 'Suppose our father was a stone in the sea,' 'Umar saw that the partnership among them in the third (of the inheritance) should be divided equally among them, males and females, and this case was called al-musharaka, or al-mushtaraka, or al-himariyya, or al-hajariyya, or al-yamiyya (1).
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage describes another type of ijtihad among the Companions: reconciling between the Quran and the Sunnah when they appear to conflict. The example is al-musharaka, a specific inheritance case. The Quran (4:12) gives one-sixth to a single sibling from the mother, and one-third divided among multiple siblings from the mother. The hadith 'Give the prescribed shares to their rightful owners' implies that full siblings (from both parents) are 'nearest male relatives' who get the remainder after prescribed shares. But what if there are both full siblings and half-siblings from the mother? The verse gives the half-siblings (from mother) a share, and the hadith gives the full siblings the remainder. 'Umar ruled that they all share the one-third equally, because the full siblings are stronger but should not be unjust; they join with the weaker half-siblings. Various objections were raised, like 'What if their father was a donkey?' (implying the full siblings have no stronger claim) or 'What if their father was a stone?' but 'Umar maintained equal partnership among all siblings. This ruling became known by several names. The passage shows that jadal (debate) occurred over this, and the companions used reasoning to harmonize texts.

Key Terms
المشركة
التوفيق

C) Because the full siblings should be just and share with the weaker.
D) Because the hadith gives everything to the nearest male relative.

Key Takeaway
Umar's ruling in al-musharaka demonstrates that reconciling texts often requires a principled reasoning that prioritises justice over literal application.
